TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT, AND CURE.
rpHE directions contained in this little treatise will be
found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous
or Sexual Debility, jVo Medicines will he required, as, by
observing the directions given, the patient can Cure Him-
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz. :~Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OP NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILIT\ r
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike any
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying
the testimonials published in his treatise. The treatise
will be forwarded on receipt of two stamps to cover

Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.— Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,'
the Capital of West Australia,

SEND FOR Dr PERRY’S FAMOUS WORK ON DISEASES OF THE REPRODUCTIVE ORGANS, FREE ON APPLICATION.
SELF- 3VC-A-IT-A.G!-E3VrEITT_
consult Dr. PERRY, M.R.C.S., L.S.A., etc.
(REGISTERED BY THE MEDICAL BOARD OF NEW SOUTH WALES),
Late House Surgeon to tin* Great Northern Hospital, Caledonian Road, London,
SPECIALIST in the Treatment of Diseases of the
Reproductive Organs and Nervous .
Dh. PERRY was induced to publicly advertise himself as a
Specialist in this branch of the profession in. consequence of the number
of persons suffering from diseases of this nature remaining nnciued ; and
this is not surprising, when it is remembered, in consequence of the
general dislike of the profession to undertake these cases, the public
health has suffered, because the majority of persons advertising to treat
these diseases, and calling themselves Doctors, Professors, etc.,
Are Neither the One nor the Other,
and exist chiefly because DULY QUALIFIED MEDICAL PRAC-
TITIONERS have not occupied the field.
Patients can, therefore, readily see the advantage of being treated
by a practitioner who lias gone through a thorough Medical, Surgical and
Anatomical Training in the best hospitals ; and the Doctor argues that,
although it may be considered contrary to antiquated medical etiquette
to so advertise, yet he is promoting the usefulness and the dignity of
the profession more by searching out and relieving this class of disease,
than by leaving them severely alone to the care of nien who, however good
their intentions, have neither the opportunities or advantages of a regular
medical training to become acquainted with the best methods of treating
these complex forms of disease.
mj
‘ : i>
ZDr.
Will be happy to advise persons suffering from these
diseases, and as, in many cases,
Nothing but the observance of a few simple directions
as to Self-Management
is required to place the patient in a healthy state, all who

SATURDAY, AUGUST 23, 1890.
Trades-Unions and Constitutional
Methods.
The Trades and Labour Councils of the
■several Australian colonies are each the execu-
(ives of Labour in the provinces where they
operate. They can scarcely be regarded as
Labour Parliaments, because, unlike most
Parliaments, they do much work and
comparatively little talking. Their chief
functions are executive and diplomatic. They
conduct, on behalf of the people whom they
represent, negotiations. They make treaties.
In one particular they have assumed a func-
tion which in most constitutional monarchies
is an appendage of the monarchs. Being
purely democratic, they exercise sovereign
rights. They declare war and make peace.
Unrecognised by the actual constitutions of
the communities in which they flourish, they
are nevertheless political entities. It has
been only of late that their organisation has
been so matured and consolidated as to in-
vest them with the far-reaching influence
which they now wield and exert. But to-day
they constitute practically an unconstitutional
power operating independently of the organic
constitution, and in some degree in antagon-
ism to it. It is remarkable that these
Labour executives have in one very signifi-
cant particular- proved themselves more
effective than the recognised parliamentary
institutions. While the latter have been
making feeble and tentative attempts at
Intercolonial Federation, the Trades and
Labour Councils have practically effected a
federation of the Union Labour of Austra-
lasia, and have even overpassed that limit
by approximating to a federation with British
Labour organisations.
* # #
It requires no special endowment of pro-
phetic gifts to perceive that the Executive of
Labour must, sooner or later, in the exercise
of its authority, come into direct conflict
with the Parliamentary fcxecutives of the
different colonies. As matters stand there
exists a radical basis of antagonism. The
regular Executive is charged with the carry-
ing out, and is sworn to execute, the laws as
t hey stand. Now, the existing code of laws -
is crammed with enactments put upon the
•Statute-book in the direct interest of Capital
and Property. Even common law, which
derives its potentiality from no statute but
from ancient custom, comes to us from times
and conditions when the classes were every-
thing and the masses nothing. Indeed it is
not necessary to go far back to find a time
when the classes were less than nothing and
worse than nonentities. “That vile and
mischievous beast, the People,” was the
phrase -current in aristocratic circles when
the Netherlands were struggling to throw oft’
the yoke of Philip of Spain, husband of
M.vuv of England. .And although the ex-
pression is to-day muttered rather than
shouted or embodied in despatches, the
sentiment survives. The regular Parliament
is all that is respectable and sagacious. But
the Executive of Labour is disreputable
and dangerous, and if not checked and con-
trolled will prove infinitely “ vile and
mischievous.” Any ordinary reader of the
capitalist daily press will recognise this tone
pervading its articles.
* * »
No rational being now cherishes the delu-
sion that Labour can be definitely arrested
on its march towards wresting from Capital
a more equitable proportion of the product
of its exertion than has hitherto been doled
out do it, unless recourse be had to actual
physical violence and a bloody internecine
conflict. Of the two rival institutions, the
Labour Executive and the regular Parliament
—both being representative bodies—either
one must prevail or both must blend. All
organic governing bodies are based, in reality,
upon actual physical power. It may be
necessary to dig deep to arrive at the founda-
tions, but without physical strength there is
no real power in any institution, however
ancient it may be, however lofty and thick
its towers and battlements, and however
substantial its appearance. In New South
Wales, at this moment, 50,000 able-bodied
men, resolute in their purposes, look to the
Trades and Labour Council for guidance.
This is a mass too mighty for the police
to cope with, and even the handful of
military hirelings which, contrary to all
democratic principle, the State main-
tains, would be impotent to control such
a multitude. But there are over two
hundred and fifty thousand more adult males
in the colony whose inclinations and sym-
pathies cannot be accurately gauged, and
behind the police and the soldiers stands the
portentous shadow of the armed might of the
British Empire—the reserve forces of Law
and Order in Australia. In short, Labour
has nothing but calamity to expect should it
come into violent conflict with the authori-
ties. Should strikers overleap the existing
code of laws in any material particular,
Capital is entitled to invoke the intervention
of the Government, and the Government
would be faithless to its oaths of office were
it to refrain from “sending the Nordenfeldts
to Newcastle ” —from setting the machinery
of repression and punishment in motion.
It is not, therefore, only those hostile to
the claims of Labour w r ho appeal to them to
proceed with deliberation and caution, to
exercise a strong ’self-restraint, and to seek
for remedies for the evils they complain of
by constitutional methods. But the coun-
sellors of Labour are apt to smile with bitter
scorn when urged to have recourse to “con-
stitutional methods ” in order to achieve
their objects. They retort upon their ad-
visers that constitutional methods have been
tried and exhausted, and nothing has come
of them. Attempts to put forward distinctively
“ Labour ” candidates for parliamentary seats
have come to nought. The personal jealousies
among the leaders, and of the leaders by the
workers in general, have proved a formid-
able. and. indeed, an insuperable obstacle
at the outset. Moreover, Trades-unionists,
although collectively powerful, are in but few
constituencies sufficiently compacted to carry
in candidates of their own nomination. And
the ordinary politicians who are apt at court-
ing the union-vote at election time by vague
expressions of sympathy, and declarations of
good intentions, have almost without excep-
tion proved unstable. The very rottenest
of reeds have indeed been the working-man
candidates.. No sooner are these fairly
ushered within the portals of Parliament
than their very natures appear to undergo a
metamorphosis. They change exteriorly, *
interiorly—physically and mentally. They
affect chimney-pot hats and frock-coats.
They commence almost at once to ape
gentility, and concentrate their efforts upon
the object of emancipating, themselves for-
ever from the thraldom of wage-earning, in
order to enter the more comfortable ranks of
the tradesman or employer. They become
eminently “ respectable,” and are at pains to
emphasise their “ moderation.” Ordinarily
a labour-“ agitator ” sent into Parliament is
in most cases lost to the cause. This result
may be attributed principally to the inter-
mittent and accidental nature of the
influence exerted at elections by the
working-class. It is only by chance, and
under exceptional circumstances, that they
manage to shove in a man of their own
choice. Once in Parliament, such a man
quickly appreciates his situation. He feels
no assurance that those who got him in can
keep him in. He sets himself to so comport
himself as to secure his return by a general
vote instead of by a class vote. He has
learned how impotent the labour-vote really
is.
There is but one remedy 'for this im-
potence, and for its consequences. The
vote of the masses—which will be in practice
the labour-vote—must be a corded its full
value. At the present time the mere people
are deluded and fooled by a spurious “ man-
hood suffrage.” Every man has a vote, but
a particular class of men have seve al votes.
The population may be roughly divided into
two classes—the wage-earner, who has a vote,
and the property-holder, who has as many
votes as lie owns hundred-pound allotments
in different constituencies It is the hitter
who is so fond of inviting Labour to' seek
remedies for its grievances “ by constitutional
methods.” And when he proflbrs this advice
he puls his tongue in his cheek, knowing
that it is in his power, by virtue of his
property, to neutralise any effort Labour
may make to secure a Parliamentary repre-
sentative to its mind, or to keep out a
distinctly antagonistic candidate. It is
not too much to affirm that were every
one of the 50,000 trades-unionists in
New South Wales to record his vote at a
general election, every one of such votes
would be neutralised and cancelled by a
plural vote given by an allotment-holder.
This does not involve the existence of fifty
thousand separate owners of allotments.
Ten thousand men, each owning five allot-
ments in different electorates, are equal in
voting power to fifty thousand men possess-
ing but one vote each ! And when it is con-
sidered that there are men who possess
property votes in as many as thirty different
constituencies, it will be comprehended that
even five thousand men of the employer
class in the metropolis may be in a position
to counterbalance the direct political power
of all the Trades-unionists in the colony.
* * #
The very first step, therefore, which
Trades-unionists and its sympathisers should
take with the view of rendering it feasible
to accomplish their aims by “ constitutional
methods” should be to concentrate all their
political energies upon securing the passing
of a Bill to abolish plural voting. This is
the key of the position. As was pointed out
in these columns last week, the wage-earning
class in New South Wales numbers about
317,000. The actual number of electors
upon the existing rolls is not so many. It is
too late this year to attend to registration,
otherwise the first political duty of the
Trades and Labour Council would be to see
that every Trades Unionist had his name
entered upon the roll of some electorate.
Evidently there has been grievous laxity in
this particular. But, as matters stand, the
full influence of the I nions should be exerted
in pressing upon members of Parliament the
necessity for passing forthwith a Bill to limit
the electoral right to One Man One Vote.
Mr. W. H. Traill has already a measure to
effect this before the Legislative Assembly.
If nothing more comprehensive can be
secured, this Bill should be passed into law.
But it would be preferable that such pressure
be brought to bear upon the Government
that they should find it imperative to them-
selves to deal with the matter on a more
comprehensive scale. As a private member,
Mr. Traill has embodied as much as could
be judiciously attempted in his Bill. He has
admitted that the absence of any provision
for registration renders his Bill imperfect in
a sensible degree. But arrangements for
registration would involve a charge upon the
revenue, and a Bill containing clauses to that
effect could not be introduced except under
cover of a message from the Governor.
This could not be procured without
the acquiescence of his Excellency’s con-
stitutional advisers—the Ministry of the
day. The present Government has op-
posed to Mr. Traill’s Bill the most un-
compromising hostility, and they and their
supporters voted to throw it out on the
second reading. Their plea was that the
Government had itself- this session—
accepted the principle, and had embodied it
in a comprehensive new Electoral Bill, which
was promised in the Governor’s speech. But
the measure of their sincerity may be ascer-
tained by considering the fact that Mr.
M’Millan only last week declared that after
the Crown Rents Bill, the Coal Mining Bill,
the Public Works Bill; the Federation reso-
lutions and the Local Government Bill had
been dealt with, the Government would
bring down their financial proposals and
dissolve the Assembly. The full political
power of Labour should be concentrated
upon an effort so to influence mem-
bers on both sides of the House, but
especially upon the Government side,
that the Ministry should at once in-
troduce, not a complicated measure such
as they have in reserve —a bill to re-distribute
seats and introduce woman-suffrage, and
other complicated trouble and tomfoolery—
but a concise enactment to cancel plural
voting, to provide for registration and voting
tickets available wherever the elector chanced
to be at the time of an election, and t > apply
it to the existing rolls, so far at least as in-
validating non-residential votes is concerned.
Should the Government not frankly yield to
the instances which would be brought into
operation, then support should be trans-
ferred instantly to Mr. Traill’s Bill, which
is good as far as it goes, and is susceptible,
if well supported, of being made applicable
to the next general election.
# * *
It is essential that the Legislature should
be made truly representative of all classes
in their numerical proportion, in order that
the cause of Labour shall achieve substantial
advances. In every modern community the
few ride on the shoulders of the many and
pick the pockets of these beasts of burden.
The plunder thus gained is used to buy bits
and bridles to put in the jaws of the luckless
Common People. The vi ry wealth which is
accumulated by appropriating from the
multitude of toilers, week after week, a
proportion of their just dues, is, when in-
vested in landed property, converted into a
pretended title to votes, which are cast
against the parties plundered whenever they
endeavour, “by constitutional methods,” to
shake their riders oft’ their {shoulders,,, and to
wrench their hands out of their pockets. It is
by such devices only that a mere hand-
ful of exploiters are enabled to enthrall
a whole population. Holding, by such
tricks, control of the Legislature, they are in
a position to shape tire laws so as to consoli-
date their advantage, and to hire, with the
funds drawn by taxation from the general
public, policemen to club strikers into
docility, and soldiers to intimidate them by
menaces of massacre with Gatling-guns. By
an intelligent comprehension of the facts,
followed by a determined application of the
remedy, it should be quite possible to reverse
the conditions, and to make the workers the
constitutional controllers of the police and
directors of the Gatling-guns.
Labour and Capital.
In the present Australian conflict between
Labour and Capital, abundant evidence shows
that both combatants see into the future.
The light no longer centres on a named
amount of money to be paid for a given
amount of work or time spent in working.
The efforts of Labour to amalgamate the
forces of its various branches, and of Capital
to consolidate its resources, take front place.
The existing dispute between steamship-
owners and the officers in their service is not
absolutely or directly a dispute about wages,
but over the fact that the officers claim the
right of affiliating themselves as a branch of
Labour with the other branches governed
by a general Labour Council. Labour desires
to draw the officers into its fold, k no whig
that with them it will be one arm stronger ;
Capital desires to keep them aloof, with the
twofold purpose of depriving Labour of an
accession of strength, and of retaining in
isolation, and consequently in comparative
helplessness, those whom it employs.
* * *
In this and a number of other current dis-
putes the question of wages is practically set
aside. But a glance ahead will show that it
is only momentarily so. Fair wages for fair
work constitute the Asses’ Bridge of econo-
mics. Since the inception of Trades-unionism,
and probably since the first day on whicli
one man employed another, the problem of
what amount of money or its equivalent pays
in full for a certain amount of toil of hand
or brain, has received the intense attention
of at least the persons concerned ; but there
stands the Asses’ Bridge to-day, without
even an approach to a permanently success-
ful solution in view. Individual employers
who attempted to solve the question by
cutting down wages provoked retaliations
which proved that no solution was possible
in that direction, and individual trades whicli
attempted the' task by forcing wages up,
either w 7 on a little advantage, which but
whetted their appetites for more, or brought
down on themselves a defeat which made
bad worse. Un neither side was there the
revelation of a principle on which to rely.
The employer and the employed were equally
hungry. Each put in motion whatever
strength opportunities gave him to take from
the other, but with no explicit conception as
to where the ability to take was to be limited
by the right to take.
* * *
On this account the strikes of the past,
whichever way they went, are valueless as
contributions to a solution of the wages
question. They only show where the
strength of numbers won or the power of
gold won, and neither evolves the principle on
which to stake a sense of right. Inasmuch
as the general tendency has been in favour
of Labour, Democracy rejoices, but it does
so, not because it accepts the methods em-
ployed in the respective battles of the
various trades, but because, seeing behind it
generations of blind toilers who stumbled,
starved, to pauper graves, it is ready to hell)
Labour to put an end to its cycle of misery
by any means—even by the morally-worth-
less argument of numbers. The wages ques-
tion is, then, still there, and its obscuration
by that of affiliation is merely temporary.
Labour has although a winner of vic-
tories in some trades, been a loser of
battles in others, and, observing the
methods which won the victories, it desires
to apply these to the contests of the future.
A particular guild forced its employers to the
wall and wrested from them a surrender ; an
amalgamation of all guilds, one vast guild,
Labour personified, proposes now to do w r ith
all employers what successful guilds occasion-
ally succeeded in doing with some.
* * *
On the other hand, employers have Oeen
observant. They saw where they lost, they
saw where they won, and have not alone
profited by past experience, but have taken
in the plan of campaign now devised
by Labour. They not alone group
themselves together, but impede where
they can the affiliation of their economic
foes. This case of the steamship-owners
and their officers illustrates the latter
phase. In the eye of Labour every man
who gives up his weeks and his years to
do the work of another as that other directs
is as much a labourer as the man who builds
a wall or digs a ditch. His wearing better
clothes makes no difference. Whether ho
walks before the mast or behind the
mast matters not one jot. He gives the
work of his life for the pay of his employers,
and the permanent interests of his class lie
not with the capitalists, but with those
who also have to give up their lives for
pay. A writer in one of the daily papers
puts forward the plea that officers are,
or should be, “gentlemen,” and their
affiliation with the seamen they direct
or with labour societies generally would
detract from their digni.y and shorten the
arm of their authority. Possibly this would


. some cases result. But Labour has in its
I t^er years been confronted with prospects
greater calamities than these and has
assed on, and no disaster has occurred
P stay her absorption of a class of
ell because some of them wholly rely on
Adventitious circumstances to support “dig-
nity ”or wield authority. Steamship-owners
and other employers who try to impede the
arC h of events by considerations of dignity
And the exercise of a verbal authority are
doin'' worse than trying to stop the incoming
tide with a broom. Labour’s citadel stands
bold and high ; every man whose hands are
hard nr whose brain is tired with the work
AAf another sees that his post is there. To
that citadel he will go ; it is a mere question
f time. - And when Capital finds that tricks
of speech will not prevail, back to its citadel
it will rush with all its speed, and then the
battle of the world begins. #
The gold of the world will be on one s de,
the labour of the world on the other ; the
universal Trust against the universal
Boycott. And what will happen '! In the
exercise of its power of numbers Labour
will commit excesses which will excite
mutiny in its ranks, for among its soldiers
will be some who own capital; the Trust,
too, will develope mutinies, for much of the
rrold with which it wages war will be drawn
from the pockets of shareholders who are
poor. With mutinies on both sides, both
sides will be forced to pause. Then will
come the day for a new social philosophy or
for the practical application of the philosophy
0 f Co-operation at present so imperfectly
understood. It may—it will —be found that
work can never be adequately paid for by
wages, and that the only solution of that
Asses’ Bridge of economics will be, like the
Gordian knot’s, its final abolition. But the
conditions precedent must be that Capital
will have realised that human sinews and
human brains have more right to rule the
world than gold has, and that Labour will
have recognised that its share in the profits
of work is not to be wholly determined by
how much it can wrest from employers by
the mere strength of numbers in organisa-
tion. To this issue Capital and Lsoour now
quickly move.
Foreign Capital.
When the Australian rises to express his patri-
otic sentiments on some occasion of public re-
joicing, he seldom fails to congratulate himself
on the influx of foreign capital, and in the fact
that his country is being “ rapidly opened up.”
The opening up of the country is in particular a
cause of much happiness, and the term serves
as a pleasant disguise to the circumstance that
an immense area of public land has been sold
for a song to speculators who will afterwards
re-sell it to the people for ten times the original
price ; while the “ influx of foreign capital” is
an agreeable synonym for the fact that the re-
sources of this new country are being used up
to pay huge dividends to foreign stock-jobbers.
The report recently published that a London
jeweller has purchased 500,000 acres at Roebuck
Bay, Western Australia, seems to suggest that
this process of national development is taking
an unexpected spurt. It is true that no one,
except the jeweller himself (who lias never
set foot on Australian soil), is likely to be
any the better for this enterprise, and when
that gentleman cuts up his great estate
some years hence, and sells it at an exorbi-
tant rate to small settlers, and takes the
proceeds to England, and the settlers borrow
the means to pay for the property from some
British mercantile and financial syndicate doing
business in the province, and spend the best
years of their lives in a toilsome endeavour to
enable the syndicate to pay dividends—in
London—of 25 per cent., and are finally fore-
closed upon, one by one, and the land resold,
and the syndicate re-organised, there will be but
little in the way of actual prosperity to show
for all these exertions. Rut still there will
remain the blessed consciousness that the
country has been largely “ opened-up ” —like a
stuck-pig—and even when a territory wide
enough to form a small republic lias been sacri-
ficed for a trifle, and lias been repurchased a
dozen times over, only to remain after all in the
hands of some foreign pawnbroking institution,
nothing can deprive us of the joyous satisfac-
tion which attends the opening-up process in all
its branches.
♦ * *
The first business of capital is to multiply
itself by taking hold of any and every enter-
prise which promises a large profit, and the first
business of the profit thus obtained is to
re invest itself and keep on increasing. Conse-
quently, the “influx of foreign capital” means
that the foreign capitalist is exerting himself to
remove from this country to his own everything
that he can get possession of, and after the same
fashion the influx of the locust, the grasshopper,
and the potato-bug are equally matters of con-
gratulation. They all take everything' they can
seize, and then go. But the presence of the
foreign capitalist lends a certain air of fictitious
prosperity to the country. New cities are built
on land owned by a British syndicate and with
money borrowed from some other syndicate;
new mines arc opened up and their products are
sent away to gladden the soul of the British
speculator with expansive dividends ; and great
tracts of country are bought and sold and mort-
gaged and financed upon, and floated into com-
panies, and organised, and re-organised, and
liquidated, and subjected to various other
expensive processes, while the prosperity of the
country advances by giant strides, and the man
who only owned .til in the past now possesses
£lOO, and ow es £99 of i( to a syndicate which
will presently sell him up And meanwhile
this prosperity lightens the heart of the
Australian citizen, and when some new British
speculator buys up a whole province in order
that he may block the progress of settlement
for a number of years, and then swindle the
coming settler, and carry away the proceeds,
there is a general feeling that the country is
looking up, and that an era of unusual progress
is setting in. And if progress consists in a
desperate effort to sell on everything that can
he sold in the present, and not to care a darn
about the future, then the term is admirably
appropriate, and expresses the situation as
nothing else can. The existing condition of
things resembles nothing so much as the tem-
porary affluence of a man who has just sold his
oidy suit of clothes at a good figure, and remains
at home naked with the money. The joyous
disregard of all save the possibilities oi the
moment is about equal in either case.
* « *
The United States has enjoyed a large experi-
ence of the blessings of foreign capital, and the
Congress at Washington is now considering the
advisability of enacting that none save an
American citizen shall be qualified to possess
land within the territories of the Union. In
America, as in Australia, the British speculator
has been acquiring enormous estates which are
of no possible benefit to the nation while he
holds them, and which only serve to impoverish
the country when, through the labour of others,
he is enabled to sell them at an immense profit
and depart with the proceeds, and there is a
growing inclination to suppress absentee land-
lordism with a strong hand. The colony of
New Zealand is now practically owned by two
immense financial establishments, and what part
of the' country these two establishments have
not a lien over is hardly worth possessing.
They have both been intimately associated with
the progress of the nation —in other words,
they have helped to grow two blades of grass
where there was only one before, and they have
got a mortgage upon both of them. And
throughout Australia the number of similar
institutions is without number, yet seldom a
year passes without some new corporation of
absentee landlords appearing on the scene, and
where the British speculator is not his place is
generally taken by some “ representative Aus-
tralian,” whom the country supports that he
may be a tenth-rate spectre hunting for a cheap
decoration in England amid the general derision
of the miserable British peerage.
# * *
Nothing but the imposition of a ponderous
tax on all non-resident proprietors —on indi-
viduals and corporations alike —promises any
remedy for this tremendous evil. It is im-
possible to build a nation out of foreign capital,
or to construct a state out of syndicates and
financial agencies, and that species of progress
which consists in selling the resources of the
country to alien speculators, and leading a riotous
existence on the proceeds, is merely drawing
bills on futurity and trusting to Providence for
the result. What that result will be can readily
be imagined. Even now commercial stagnation
arises the instant the flow of foreign gold suffers
a temporary stoppage, for the country cannot
stand the ceaseless drain necessary to pay in-
terest on borrowed capital unless more capital
is forthcoming, to add to the load in its turn,
and necessitate still further borrowing. Every
financial society which pays dividends in London
out of Australian investments —every “repre-
sentative” colonial who leaves home to grovel to
British royalty, and exhibit his gilded ignorance
in the mother country —makes an addition to
the burden. And the absentee proprietor is an
active political evil in addition to his other sins.
He is the “bond,” above all others, which
unites Australia to the troubled destinies of the
Old World, and, like bonds in general, it is
necessary to pay interest on him. He is one of
the greatest obstacles to land reform in the
future, for his existence will make land-
nationalisation in Australasia an international
question, to be argued, it may be, as
the cause of the Egyptian bondholders was
argued a few years ago at Alexandria, and as it
bade fair to be argued a few days ago on the
coast of Guatemala. He is the incarnation of
the pawnshop and the loan-agency-—the
apotheosis of the bill-of-sale —the embodiment
on a gigantic scale of that spirit of enterprise
which makes a small advance on a shirt at <5
per slient. He exists simply that this country
may send him the interest regularly, and when
he has increased and multiplied at his present
ratio for a few years more it will become neces-
sary to send him pretty nearly the whole
country to settle his claims upon it. Mexico
was for many years a favourite field of specula-
tion, and she went broke under the infliction.
The Argentine Republic lias tried it, and the
results to-day are eminently disastrous. India
is in a woeful state of poverty under the same
infliction. Egypt has been occupied by a
British Army whose mission it is to keep
its eye on the interests of British investors, no
matter who may be killed in the process. New
Zealand has suffered ten years of abject depres-
sion in her efforts to recover from the influence
of too much foreign capital. The absentee
proprietor is everywhere objectionable, whether
he takes the form of the millionaire landholder,
who has gone home to spend his income and
make a painful exhibition of his vulgarity, or
assumes another aspect as the European, Asian,
and Australasian Investment and Mortgage Asso-
ciation, with a capital of £3,000,000 of British
money, all of which is to be ultimately repaid
tenfold by the unfortunate country which that
enterprising body proposes to exploit. Only
the latter labours under additional obloquy as
an elaborate joint-stock cormorant, whose
systematic habits of plunder probably make
him in the end a good deal the more expensive
of the two.
The Queensland M sa[?]e.
The new mosaic Ministry of Queen.. .and has
astonished both political parties and pleased
neither. Yet it is strategically strong, because
lion, members of the present Opposition, though
numerically ahead, will avoid dissolution as a
leper. The country has become so disgusted
with “the twins Moreheau and Pattisox
—and their tail, that, were an appeal made to
the constituencies straight away, the new
oki kkith-MTr avkaith combination would carry
all before it. Disappointed Groom can never
head a formidable third party, and the days of
Darling Downs-cnwi-Ipswioh “bunches” are
over. A dissolution just now would simply
send Griffith, MTtavkaith and Co. “in”
for a five-years’ good chance ; whereas,
if they make any mistakes after a while,
or if certain very possible contingencies
happen, a dissolution may be “safer” in
one or two years than it now presents itself to
a perturbed lot of recipients of £25 sterling
per month. The Ministerial combination cer-
tainly is curious. The late MTlwraithians
say that it is that hateful Mte noir of their’s—
“ a Queen-street Governmentwhile the late
Griffithites profess to abhor the action of their
late demi-god, Sir Samuel, in completing an
unholy alliance with such a “fiat contra-
diction” as MTlwraith, and such a cham-
pion of that execrable tiling, Black Labour,
as Cowley. Let us look into things. Out
of a Ministry of eight, there are certainly
five who may fitly be described as ‘ ‘ Queen-
street men ” —namely, Griffith, MTlwraith
(as member for North Brisbane, though he
has squatting and mining and meat-preserving
and commercial interests in many parts of
Queensland), Unmack (a Brisbane accountant
and member for the suburb of Toowong), T. J.
Byrnes (a young Brisbane barrister), and
W. H. Wilson (a Brisbane solicitor). The
other three may be thus written down: —
Tozer, a Gympie mining lawyer, with slight
interests in Charters Towers, Rockhampton,
and (probably) Ipswich ; Cowley, a Northern
planter, and nothing else ; Hodgkinson, an ex-
oflicial of cosmopolitan experience and who is
like Paul in the matter of being ‘ ‘ all things to
all men,” but Northern, because a Gulf repre-
sentative. By the way, the disgusted ex-
Griffithites need not, after Sir Samuel’s blend-
ing with the formerly ultra-MTlwraithian
Hodgkinson, have been surprised at the not
very much greater somersault of the present
combination.
* # *
So long as the Mosaic Ministry keeps ‘ ‘ white ”
with the people on the question of black labour,
it will remain in a strong position. And neither
Griffith nor MTlwraith is likely to call up

PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Ominous Apparition.
This borrowed Avail is republished in Melbourne
Stanhaud as a humble ottering to Australian
Toryism
The national governments of the present day find them-
selves confronted by the ominous apparition of an intel-
lectual demon which their own spell has invoked, and,
like the bungling necromancer of mediiuval fame, they are
now puzzled as to the proper means to resort to m order
to lay it. The demon is the demon of Socialism ; it is only
intellectual in the sense of its being the creature of an
erroneous system of education, for which these govern-
ments are responsible.
Air “erroneous system ot education ’ in this
case obviously means any system of education
which enables the world to realise what a poor,
mean lot its rulers really are, and which tends
to diminish its reverence for that glorious thing
known as the Throne. The Socialist suffers
from an objectionable idea that it is absurd to
grovel in adoration before an expensive fetich,
simply because that fetich is hired at
an enormous cost to do something that he
generally has not brains enough to do in any
case, and which the nation could easily do
itself at a hundredth part the cost and with all
the grovel left out, and which also might be
left undone without anyone being at all the
wiser. Ho has been -created by education,
because man requires to know something before
he is able to recognise the fact that his rulers
don’t know anything in particular, and the
education was no doubt “erroneous” in this
sense, for it is an idiotic error in any class that
wants to hang on to its fat offices and its land-
steals and other perquisites, to allow the nation
which it livqs upon to learn that it can do
better without it. It is also a miserably erro-
neous thing to let the country know that it
doesn’t need an aristocracy, and that a landlord
is not essential to happiness, and that a monarch
is no earthly use, and that a Gold-Stick-ill -
Waiting and a Master of the Horse, and an
Established Church, and an old, wheezy Com-
mander-in-Chief, are collectively of no more
service than a monkey sitting on a stump ;
and with a view to preventing a general
knowledge of these things, the Governments
who are harassed by the v apparition ” in
question, diffused alxmt as little education as
they could. The mistake which promises to
undo them is that they failed to recognise what
a very small amount of education is required
to capsize the old and what a
tremendous amount of ignorance is needed to
prop them up. A population which is far
enough advanced to spell “dog” without an
“r” is getting to be filled up with dangerous
information, and is liable at any moment to lose
its reverence for monarchy and the laws of pro-
perty and the House of Peers and the vicar and
the local squire, and the C.M.G., and when this
reverence is gone the Government may look for
the arrival of an “ ominous apparition ” at any
moment.

Gagged!
Under its present Tory management the Aus-
tralian Natives’ Association might as well be
the Primrose League, or a Vice-Regal Protec-
tion Society. Beyond a perpetual license to
love the Queen with a loud voice, and a special
permit to “tighten those silken bonds,” &c.,
the organized son of Australia enjoys'no birth-
right advantages over the humble earth-worm,
or creeping lizard. His chief article of Associa-
tion is the National Anthem, and his political
aspirations are controlled by a board of directors
who delight to chain him up in the way ho
shouldn't go. If, as occasionally happens, he.
developed a strange yearning for the infinite
and >a practical desire to open out, then imme-
diately the iron paw of a special committee lays
hold upon his soul and wipes the floor with it.
Recently the Collingwood (Melb.) Branch of
Natives arranged to meet together and hear
what one Rosa, “ the unemployed agitator,”
had to say about Socialism. There is no
apparent reason why Rosa shouldn’t be allowed
to chip in his remarks on Socialism as freely as
Lord Hopktoux— another unemployed agitator
—is permitted to go drivelling around concern-
ing the blessings of a constitutional form of
government which includes a House of Peers.
The one appears to be representing men who
want to work for a living, while the other
is an official mouthpiece of the classes who can
live without wanting to work at all. Anyhow,
the Collingwood Natives had consented to give
Rosa a fair hearing when the supreme President
of the Association wrote to say they mustn’t,
because the idea was “preposterous,” and
“beyond all limits” fixed by the grovelling
guardians of young Australian thought. By
way of salve for Collingwood’s bruised manhood,
however, several members of the Board of Direc-
tors have volunteered to substitute for the ad-
dress on Socialism a spirited lecture on Capital,
or a paper on the Blessings of Free Discussion.
A Bash Undertaking'.
New Zealand, like New South Wales, possesses
an Upper Chamber of nominees who can only be
'•ot rid of by the kindly assistance of death, and
she is at last beginning to realise that they don’t
die fast enough to be of much 'service. Conse-
quently Colonel Whitmore has introduced a
scheme for the amendment of the Constitution
by limiting the term of the irresponsible legis-
lator to 10 years, and he hopes by this measure
to get rid of him before he has turned com-
pletely bad. From this it will readily be
observed that the Colonel is of a sanguine turn
of mind. In almost every case the member of
the Upper House is prematurely-old and men-
tally exhausted when he begins his career.
There is no excitement, no glory, and no show
of usefulness in his position, and he accepts it
chiefly because it offers him the use of a calm
spot where he can cast anchor and moulder
away in peace. The country pays him to
moulder, and he does it. He is nominally
appointed for the purpose of checking hasty
legislation, but if he is found attempting
it. measures are generally taken to make
him stop. He is supposed to legislate
a little on his own account, but when
he makes any effort in that direction
he makes such unsutferable confusion that the
elective chamber brings him up short. It is
also his business to keep a sober, unbiassed eye
on the public expenditure, but he knows better
than to try any such experiment: for if he were
caught doing it he would be alwffishcd. The
only thing he is allowed to do is to moulder
officially and propound venerable theories lioru
of the solemn calm and old fogey ism of his sur-
roundings, and under these circumstances it is
a matter of comparative indifference whether i o
moulders for 10 years or tor life Duly as tho
conditions of Government require that lmshould
be squashed whenever he tries t.o do anything
that the Lower House wouldn't have done,
equally well without him, it is perhaps ad vis
able that he should be as old and senile as
possible, so as to facilitate the squashing process.

a man, or a body of men, should habitually
tread on another, it is folly to seek to
infuse extra vigour into the trodden-on
party. If New Zealand intends to adhere
to the nominee system she had better be
content with the inoffensive fossil she has
got, instead of trying to raise up a compara-
tively young and vigorous fossil which might
give trouble. If she should decide instead to
have a Council elected by the people on a
liberal basis, she will get a body which will be
a mere reflex and duplicate of the Assembly,
and in that case it would be just the same
thing to have no Council at all; and if she
should adopt the Victorian principle, and elect
the Upper House on a property qualification,
she will be merely organising the cause of
Capital and old Toryism, and placing it in a
position to fight against the democratic tend-
encies of the. peoples’ representatives. The
Upper House, in short, should be let alone
till the nation has time to abolish it. Its
mission is to inspect and review the reckless
acts of the popular Chamber, but so long as
it understands that it will be killed on the spot
if it is found attempting to execute its mission
in any practical way its presence doesn’t matter
so very much. But considering the amount of
trouble which a new colony experiences before
it succeeds in calming down the Upper Obstruc-
tion into a fitting state of uselessness, it is
something approaching insanity to attempt to
rouse it up again, and make fresh difficulties.
The New Zealand House of Lords has been in a
reasonably torpid and frozen state for some
years past, and the country has enough on its
hands at present without endeavouring to throw
it out.
The Dyed-Haii' Man.
We should imagine that a perusal of “ Darkest
Africa ” would be sufficient to convey, even to
the most fatuous of Stanley’s admirers, some
idea of the real littleness of their hero. Major
Bartelott, who was murdered, comes in for a
full measure of censure, especially for his atro-
cious wickedness in sending away Stanley’s
baggage (containing the celebrated hair-dye).
It is on this that Stanley especially harps when
referring to Troup and Bonny. The idea of
his own infallibility is what he strives to
impress on the reader throughout the book.
The fiasco of the rear-guard he ascribes
to “ a supernatural malignant influence at
work to thwart honest intention,” and,
although he does not say so in as many
words, evidently insinuates that reading the
Bible twice through was a considerable help to
success. Of the nigger-hunting and shooting
that went on we have a sufficiently full account,
the responsibility of it being calmly ascribed to
God. The gallant Captain Chari.es Sturt,
whom Australians leave unhonoured, wrote at
the end of his last journal; ‘‘ My path amongst
savage tribes has been a bloodless one, not but
that I have often been placed in situations of
risk and danger, when I might have been justi-
fied in shedding blood, but I trust I have ever
made allowances for human timidity, and res-
pected the customs of the rudest people.” How
do these modest and manly words read beside
the nauseous egotism of that blushless self-
advertiser, Brigand Stanley, of the dyed hair ?
More Sorts of Govex’nment.
A fancy illustration of party Government—
which ought to come in neatly somewhere after
The Bulletin’s recent comments on Govern-
ment by Contradiction—is to hand from Queens-
land, which is now r venturing upon an un-
equalled reconciliation of old and violent foes.
Wheii an innocently-accused man leaves the
criminal dock he doesn’t often forgive the man
w'ho was at once his false accuser and his mali-
cious prosecuting lawyer. Yet Sir Thomas
MTlwraith and Sir Samuel Griffith stand
to each other in this precise sort of personal
relation. The former was accused of gigantic
swindles and personal pecuniary benefits, and the
latter pursued him relentlessly, causing him to
spend thousands of pounds in the defence of his
personal character, and even to undertake a
journey to England to plead before the Privy
(lounciL When Sir Thomas triumphantly
emerged from a prolonged pursuit by the clever-
est lawyer in Queensland, even though public
occasions offered, and little would have been
accepted as a full apology, Sir Samuel refused to
retract a single statement or charge preferred.
No only so refused, but brought up the “swindle”
Again and again at public opportunities and in
persistently repeated defamations. Sir Thomas
vehemently once declared that he could never
take. Sir Samuel’s hand again, as Sir Samuel
had virtually wished to place him as a felon in
a dock, and had not the manliness to attempt
to even modify his most strenuous bitterness.
Vet, Jo ! the world w'ags, and Brisbane folk are
astonished to see the two foes “ a-kebbing” it
t ogether, and—last transformation of all—they
are now' the Siamese Twins of the new Queens-
land Government. “Turn on, old Time,” and
show us some more farces.
The great Building Society case at Melbourne
police-court, should be coming to an utterly
monstrous conclusion this week. At some times
a screaming joke, at others a drunken, rowdy
farce, the case finally resolved itself into a
serious insult to the name of justice. It was on
last Tuesday morning that the law, as repre-
sented at police-courts, was knocked clean out of
time by, the weight of influential respectability
opposed to it. As a matter of plain, unvarnished
intention all the parties accused of conspiracy,
with the solitary exception of Mr. Dow , were
committed for trial. Chairman Panton stated
that the Bench, being fully empowered to end
the case there and then, had decided to dis-
charge Mr. Dow, and send everybody else
(although with great reluctance in the cases of
Ximmo, Ferguson and Stewart) to a higher
Court. Whereupon the counsel for the verbally,
but not yet formally, committed parties rose,
one after the other, and induced, persuaded, or,
as some will say, bullied the Bench into taking
back their decision until they (the counsel) had
made speeches for the defence. Next day the
Chairman took ill, and those speeches have been
delivered in his absence. Should Mr. Panton’s
original decision be changed, under such extra-
ordinary circumstances, Thk Bulletin will
endeavour to unravel the mystery next week.

- ♦
More should come of that deadlock between the
New South Wales Minister for Instruction and
the New South Wales Civil Service Board in
regard to the appointment of Mr. George
Mill eh to the position of Comptroller-General
of Local Prisons. The present stage of the
difficulty is neither satisfactory to the Minister
nor to the Board, and is eminently unsatisfac-
tory to the public at large. The Board’s
primary function is to fill up vacancies in the
Service. Parliament and the country gave it
existence for that special work. In the case
under discussion, however, the vacancy was not
reported to the Board, and the Minister’s—not
the Board’s—fiat established Mr. Miller in the
post. Minister and Board have, after dis-
courteous reference to each other, lapsed now
into stubborn silence. Why does not some
member stir the Assembly on the subject?
Wisely enough the Civil Service Act allows
appointments to lie made to the Service on
account of special qualifications which cut-and-
dry rules cannot reach; but the question is
whether a merely temporary occupant of a
Ministerial position, or whether a regularly -
constituted Board, is to be judge of this special
fitness? If the Minister, are the days of
patronage dead ?
What is to be substituted in New South Wales
for imprisonment for non-payment of libel verv
diets? The Bulletin responds that there is
no need to substitute for it anything in particu-
lar. Imprisonment for mere debt is an iniquity
—a mere survival of barbarism—and imprison-
ment for “contempt of Court,” i.e., for non-
payment of lawyers’ perquisites, is one of the
most shameless of the numerous frauds which
the land-shark guild are still privileged to
commit. The cry that to abolish imprisonment
in such cases would be to let loose on the
community “ a host of libellers,” loses all
force, when it is remembered that a man
can only be fairly imprisoned for libel when
he has been found guilty of criminal libel
after a proper trial before a jury. The
arguments used by Judge Trench in these
columns a week or two ago apply to all
alleged offences—whether against property,
person or character. When a man is charged
with libel let him, if the grand jury of the
country thinks fit to file a bill, be placed on his
trial on two counts —firstly, criminal libel, an
offence against the State; secondly, for
damages at the suit of the individual aggrieved.
Let the two cases be tried together, and let one
jury ladle-out both damages or imprisonment,
or damages and imprisonment as they may
think necessary. If the jury are shown that
the libel is a bad and deliberate one, the
offender will go to gaol for a period within the
maximum fixed by the statute, as the unfortu-
nate Reseigh Martin has gone to gaol. With
this difference, however, that you will not find
juries committing the atrocities perpetrated
under the present system of judge-made
sentences. Where would be the logic of im-
prisoning a man whom a jury had pronounced
guiltless of crime, merely because he had no
money wherewith to satisfy a civil verdict for
damages ?
John Henry Newman.
The death of John Henry Newman is not, in the
proper sense of words, a calamity or even a
bereavement. He went out at the age of 90, not
as a candle goes out when the flame reaches the
socket, not as the sun goes down when it reaches
the horizon, but as a fire, which has blazed and
smouldered its substance away, cools off into
white ash. His eyes did not see the world going
by, his ears did not hear its vehement words,
and if they heal’d, the language was as
that of a planet on which his feet had
never trod. There is reverence in the sym-
pathy which bemoans his departure, but
there are insincerity and affectation in it also.
John Henry Newman was dead long before the
cardinal’s hat was placed on his white head, or
the red stockings of ecclesiastical authority bound
his ankles. That which was great in him, that
which made him one of the first dozen English-
men of 30 years ago, had fought its last battle and
retired to rest before Pius IX. asserted the Papal
Infallibility, or Napoleon 111. handed up sword
and crown. Since that day the great priest would,
dead, have been as potent in the world’s affairs as
he was living. The demise of the other day was,
then, but a performance of the technical act of
dying, and lamentations over it arc but custom’s
tribute, itself a thing purely technical also, or
folly’s indulgence in the occupation of entertaining
itself with its own sorrows.
# * *
Fifty years ago Newman was in his prime. His
thoughts were those of a man in whose being the
roots of immortality had struck deep, and the
fragrance and flowers of the evergreen tree intoxi-
cated his senses. Bird and beast, and earth and
sky, all told him of his soul; history, ancient and
modern, told him of it; tyrannies, pestilences,
and famines told him of it; and the murder of
man by man told him of it. The world turned on
its axis or revolved about the sun, and every sign
of the zodiac spoke to him of his soul. What
could such a man do but hunt through palimpsests
and parchments for directions on the matter, once
he accepted the premise that directions on the
topic were given to man ? Newm&n’s 1 1 ands turned
pagei? endlessly, his eyes were ceaseless interroga-
tions of persons and events. His sermons wei*
queries, his explanations investigations. In no
man was the union between head and heart closer,
the head speaking through the heart and the heart
beating in the head, but thought and throb went
all to soul, and to soul only. When the fight was
in the open, while the individual faced the uni-
verse and strove to wrest from it the secret as to
which labyrinthine doctrinal passage would lead
him out to light, the spectacle was one to inflame
enthusiasm and excite love. The battle was so
unequal, the labyrinths so numerous, and the in
dividual so insistent, with never a false syllable
from his lips, and never a quiver of despair
in his heart, that it is no wonder young
Oxford went to' his feet and ingenuous minds
everywhere studied his words. And the words in
themselves were worth study. The English
language was never used with better effect. Ab-
struse though many of his themes were, and
involved his sentences, the sense held itself
dominant and was apprehensible almost by all. In
imaginative power he was much less strong than
many of his contemporaries, but so spiritualised
was his existence that a glow akin to that of the
highest imaginative quality was shed on all his
woi’k. But when “ Kindly Light ” led him on, as
he had prayed it to do, and he put the stole of fct.
Philip Neri around his neck, the battle was over.
His movements in the direction of higher education
and his bickerings with Cardinal Cullen over the
rectorship of Dublin University, as well as his en-
counter with Gladstone on the Vatican Decrees are
all within the sphere where any ordinary mediocre
man can, if he so like, make himself look tem-
porarily great. And the Cardinal seemed to know
it. He confined himself closer to his oratory,
built higher and higher the wall between himself
and the outside world, and holding his soul in his
hands- the soul over which his life-work had been
spent—he waited for the summons to drop into
dust. If men have souls, and those souls are saved
in that way, there can be no doubt that John
Henry Newman s is now happy.

The Tintamarre.
“ Not’ Maitre, this is the Tintamarre*
Of the village of Oarmeray.”
So spoke a sunburnt campagnard
By the Beauron’s winding way.
From hand to hand, f&nn voice to voice,
Five hundred years, men say,
It has summoned the weary to rejoice
At the death of the worker’s day ;
“ Ha-ro-o!
Gilles, Jacquot.
Dieu pardoint an bon Comte Thibaut!
Ha-ro-o!
Marthe, Margot.
Dieu pardoint au bon Comte Thibaut!
Au tout bon Comte de Blois !
At the first sweet sound of the vesper-bell
The harvester drops the hay ;
And leaving the last tree where it fell,
The wood-cutter turns away.
Then he thinks how his father’s fathers toiled
From dawn to dusk of day :
And he crosses his tools in the Tintamarre,
And he bares his brow to pray.
The hurrying ploughman stops half-way
In the furrow turned for grain •
Alone, he doubles the roundelay,
And with whetstone strikes his ryain ;
The ditcher, clearing his dusty throat,
Sends on the same refrain,
Till the wandering goat-herd, note for note
Gives the Haro back again.
Still the miller reckons his empty sacks,
As he stays in the mill alone ;
Still the miserly farmers bend their backs,
For the harvest is all their own.
And—-ha !ha! ha ! “It would grieve a Turk,”
The wiseacres sighing, say,
“That the precious daylight God gave for work,
Men and women should dance away. ”
Now the fiddler’s time of toil begins,
Yet he, too, gives thanks to heaven ;
For, old and blind, he hardly wins
The scanty bread of seven.
And clattering after his dancing feet
Come the village children all,
As they mimic the sounds of the Tintamarre,
And echo the elders’ call;
“ Ha-ro-o !
Jacques, Renaud.
Dieu pardoint au bon Comte Thibaut!
Ha-ro-o !
Jeanne, Babeau.
Dieu pardoint au bon Comte Thibaut!
Au tout bon Comte de Blois !”

Still, the grandsires say, does the good Comte’s
soul
Haunt forest ahd champ and clos,
Still he claims his lordship on every bole,
And from every furrow thus takes his toll:
“ Dieu pardoint au bon Comte Thibaut!
Dieu pardoint au Comte de Blois ! ”
—Julia M. O'Ryan.
* According to a tradition, Count Thibaut, of Blois,
taking pity on the lot of those who toiled in the fields,
fixed the hour for beginning and ending the day’s work.
Every evening, when the bell of the tower had rung, one
could hear the workmen nearest to the tower warning
their fellow-toilers, either by shout or by the sound of
their picks and spades, which they struck against one
another. This was the Tintamarre, and during the con-
fused hum could be heard the grateful shouts, “ God
pardon the good Count of Blois ! Monteil.
Mildura, the irrigation settlement, contains
no pubs, and —no lawyers.

of The Bulletin and still a valued contributor to
this paper, has (out of 53.0 candidates) come out
emphatically top in bookseller Cole’s prize com-
petition for the best essays advocating the Federa-
tion of the World. As Mr. Edmond also took a
prize for his essay in opposition to world-federation
it cannot be contended that he is incapable of see-
ing both sides of a question. Should bookseller
Cole require a prize essay on the uselessness of
bishops or the inherent Primroseism of the Aus-
tralian Natives’ Association as evidenced by its
senseless adoration of the Whelp family, Mr.
Edmond will fill the bill every time. The judges
in the competition were Professor Timken and
Gosman, and Mr. Alexander Sutherland, M.A.

If the appetite of Sydney audiences for
good things lias not been prematurely satisfied, the
cosy little “ Cri.” will be uncomfortably packed
during the two weeks’run apportioned to “Fedora.”
Sardou’s play is admirably staged—though the
scene in the first act is somewhat cramped
and well impersonated, albeit one or two ot the
minor parts might be in abler hands. That Janet
Achurch’s Fedora is a fine performance is made
more thoroughly evident by the S.M. Herald’s
attempt to depreciate it by comparison with that
of the creator of the part, Sara Bernhardt. This
“ set ” at the young actress has caused some com-
ment among those who noticed how studiously
such methods were avoided in that quarter during
the Potter-Bellew season; but, doubtless, the
writer is fully justified, for any comparison
between Bernhardt and a mere imitator, would
have been ridiculous, and therefore Miss Achurch
may look on the pai-allel drawn in her case as a
great, if involuntary, compliment. Anyhow, she
gives us a very fine reading of the character; a
little overacted in gesture and facial expression in
the third act, when Ipanoff has the scene
to himself, but, nevertheless, perfectly coherent
in its rapid transitions from laughter to tears,
from vengeful pursuit to tender solicitude. George
Titheradge’s impersonation of Loris Ipanoff is one
of his best, nay, his very best, and furnishes a dis-
play of tragic power and emotion of which we did
not think him capable. The Qpuntess Olga of
Fanny Enson, though a very different character,
is up to the high standard of her Louise ; Charles
Charrington, if not brilliant, is painstaking and
correct as de Seriex; and Herbert Flemming, com-
pletely disguised as Gretch in a curly wig and
moustache, makes the most of an abbreviated
opportunity. Alice May is a very nice, though
somewhat shamefaced boy, Harry Leston’s Desire
is acceptable, Brian England’s Rouvel gentle-
manly and easy, but theßolslas Lasinskiof “ Boh”
Watson would more fitly grace “ Muldoon’s
Picnic,” and the red-bosomed Borloff of J. P.
West suggests a Greek ex-pirate who has also re-
tired—on a competence—from the ice-cream, or
oyster-and-fruit trade.

The return of the popular Maggie Moore,
skittish and in good voice, to Her Majesty’s stage
has wonderfully picked up the house ; though
“Meg” seems even greater drivel than before.
Harry Jewett is as effective as it is possible to be
when loaded with ridiculous improbabilities, and
that really wonderful child, Violet Aston (erstwhile
“ Baby ” Nidi oils) is a host in herself. As hinted
last week, “Macbeth” will be produced on the
30th. Rignold will play the name-part, the Lady
Macbeth will be —Miss Janet Achurch ! Herbert
Flemming will tackle Malcolm, and Charles
Charrington will endeavour to get away with
Macd uff. Harry Jewett may impersonate Duncan,
but more probably will take a holiday.

A change of bill at the School of Arts
causes Harry Rickards, though in his third week,
to turn away money nightly. “Pink Notes,” the
most impossible farce the Co. has yet handled, is
a howl from start to finish. Flushed with success,
Rickards talks of increasing the size of the pit by
making a new gallery entrance and seating the
stalls with 300 opera-chairs. Matinee performance
every Saturday.

The sixth week of “ Paul Jones ” opened
to a great declension of business, so great as to
make it evident that the announcement of “Boc-
caccio ” for Aug. 30 was not premature. During
the week the fair Ellen’s bronchial tubes have been
a little out of order, and one evening when the
very popular trio of the third act was encored, she
made ill-health an excuse for refusing the repe-
tition.

»* * *
The enthusiasm attending Frances Savile’s
Melbourne performances, after nearly six years’
absence, has led people there to remark that she is
not fully appreciated in Sydney. No greater
favourite ever stepped on a Sydney platform, but
as the local musical societies prefer to hire
auxiliary talent from other cities or even to utilise
incapable amateurs, rather than the high-class
professional residents, Sydney audiences rarely
hear her. The Sydney professional is always in
demand when charity performances are being
given, but, as a rule, when money is to be paid for
services, then amateur managers go further afield.
Charity begins at home, and Sydney’s professionals
are being gradually starved off to other cities.

Wolfe’s Schnapps—popularity increases yearly.
The printer who last week inserted an
advt.: “Wanted, Foster-mother for litter of collie
pups,” in the “Servants Wanted” column of a
Sydney daily, must have been reading the story
of Romulus and Remus upside-down.
Ask for Wolfe’s Schnapps and take no other.
Alma Tadema,, the artist, who is uncom-
monly fastidious about his personal apparel, lately
had as a guest at his house one Yedder, literary
Bohemian. The morning after Yedder’s arrival at
the Tadema mansion Mrs. Tadema was awakened
by a rude knocking at her chamber-door; much
alarmed, she aroused her husband, who demanded,
in fierce tones, what was wanted. It was Mr.
Yedder who was at the door, and he answered, in
a voice loud enough to be heard all through the
house; “Isay, Tadema, old chap, where do you
keep the scissors that you trim your cuffs with ?”

»# * *
Another sustained rush of paying and
dead-head public to the Opera House welcomed
the return of Widow O’Brien. Of Sheridan’s im-
mortal creation a list of stereotyped praises may
be taken as read. The little man conceals his
identity under the Widow’s robes and false front
as completely as ever. He nothing exaggerates
nor sets down aught in malice, a Shakespeare
misquotation which also applies to The Bulletin,
God bless it ! John Forde takes the place of
Kelly as the Italian noble, with advantage to the
comedy interest, but for the rest, an increase of
variety business has not done much for “Fun on
the Bristol,” beyond spinning it to a time when
its early virtues are well-nigh forgotten. Even
the Sappho dance suffers from over-elaboration,
and the Harvey Brothers, alleged to be miracles
in their way, only serve to remind us that the
Raynors were much more artistic in a similar
direction. But one of those inimitable brethren
has been playing weird instruments on the Golden
ghoah these many months, and a live Harvey is
probably more useful for worldly purposes than
a dead Raynor.
* *■ * *
Mrs. Potter has relapsed into “ Mdlle. de
Bressier,” at Melbourne Princess’s, Here again
the fair lady’s F renchy mannerisms seem to suit
the character, and as often as not she is discovered
to be doing the right thing in the right way.
Bellew likewise appeals to the dress-circle with his
average force in the capacity of the sculptor who
cannot marry Mrs. Potter until she has routed out
the corpse of her late, lamented by way of
moist, unpleasant proof that she doesn’t
meditate bigamy. “ Mdlle. de Bressier ” is
a difficult play to lay hold of and consider
calmly, as Sydney audiences are aware, but it
contains many bright gleams of bloodshed and
misery, and becoming hats, not to mention some
respectable acting by others than the beautiful
stars. Sass, Miss Williamson, Wilson, Forbes,
and Diver appear to advantage. If the last-
named young man could but conquer his nervous-
ness, and put on flesh and voice, he might paralyse
them one of these days, for the soul of the artist
evidently beats within his slim torso. There has
been some talk of producing a new classical play,
“Hero and Leander,” next Saturday, but the
dangerous experiment may not come off on that
date, in which case another agony from the
French, of which Mrs. Potter has dozens, will
probably be called for.
-:tf * * *
Business at the Royal has been brisker
since “The Lucky Lot” began to play its old
stage-tricks upon that section of the public which
loves to see villainy knocked prostrate by a meat-
axe or small blunderbuss at every critical moment.
Although the education of the classes goes inarch-
ing on, like John Brown’s knapsack, there is still
a shock-headed percentage of mass ready to accept
a foggy-voiced bandit, in well-blacked Welling-
tons and an aggravating simper, as the
personification of all that is best in British
drama. Unfortunately, however, the truth
concerning that steeplechase act is slowly
leaking forth, and as it becomes noised
abroad that George Darrell’s ride for free-
dom, with a supposititious Christina in his strong
right arm, is actually perpetrated by an experi-
enced ostler who clutches a life-sized rag-doll, the
demand for realism will probably fall off. The
conviction that George Darrell and Christina are
waiting behind the scenes meanwhile, ready to
come forward and scoop the glory that properly
belongs to a nameless equestrian and his sackful
of shreds and patches, is not elevating to the
observant mind. If it was fair to laugh at Carrie
Swain’s hona fide swim around a tank, it is fair
to snort at a sensation absurd in conception
and fictitious in the carrying out. Why not en-
gage Tommy Corrigan to make the leap, and star
that popular artist as chief performer ? Christine
Darrell’s singing has improved since the first night,
and her limelight dance--in which, after bemoan-
ing her penniless condition, she flashes brown silk
stockings and boots to match —is understood to be
a further proof of the young lady’s budding genius.
Another lively feature of “ The Lucky Lot” is a
little grassy mound, representing somebody’s grave.
No gaudy granite tombstone, or gilt railings, dis-
figure the hallowed spot, but a quantity of paper
poppies, planted on the last resting-place by loving
hands, are doosid affecting.
Dearest mother’s prayer is granted,
She is safe in Heaven’s bowers,
Where her weary bones were planted
Friends have stuck some paper flowers.
-* * * *
The presence of a valiant curate in ‘ ‘ The
Golden Ladder” is responsible for a sudden in-
flux of clergy to the Alexandra “circle.” Com-
fortable-looking shepherds beam across their
white chokers at the spectacle of the Reverend
Somebody keeping everybody at bay with a
revolver, or flourishing a thick stick around a
strong situation, while he explains that he is a
desperate man. In truth, the Alexandra drama
acts as an antidote to the “ Private Secretary,”
wherein the chosen apostle was invariably ad-
dressed as a “miserable worm,” and con-
stantly being pitched into the coal-scuttle
hy a worldly-minded uncle. One of these
nights the management will announce a
special Evangelical performance under the patron-
age of Field Flowers Goe, after which a fusty
fear of the Devil, which alone keeps many wealthy
old dodderers away from the temples of Thin-
gummy, will he melted into thin-air by the
Bishop’s abundant smile. Asa play, apart from
the splendour of its scenery, “The Golden
Ladder ” improves on acquaintance, a serio-comic
passage, dealing with the parson’s bill for board
and residence, being especially bright and human.
The one weak part in the mounting is aa office-
table, so ingeniously constructed that, with a
change of scene, it can be shifted back a few yards
and converted into a sideboard. Sometimes this
combination bit of furniture gets through the
trying ordeal all right, but sometimes the table
turns into a sideboard before its time, and every-
body wonders where the clown and pantaloon
come in. Walter Howe has displaced Rosier, for
a while, in the parson’s billet, ?md business looks
healthy.

Slade Murray’s combination of legs and
harmony seems to be established for all time at
the Gaiety Hall, the Victoria niggers have safely
negotiated another week, and the Battle of

spluttering of Gladstone, as he confers his blessing
upon Lord Carington, is heard no more in Kreit-
mayer’s Waxworks.
* * * «-
The friends of Geo. 0. Miln will be glad
to hear that his trip to New Zealand, which began
with misgivings, terminated in exceeding joy. At
various points of the two islands Miln knocked
persons of consequence into heaps of admiration.
Once he lured the Bishop into the theatre and
held him fast with his glittering; eye ; as for
Governor Onslow, he was so carried away by
Othello’s feelings that he thumped a small table in
front of him “ until a leg broke.” Whether the
leg belonged to the table, or to the vice-regal per-
son, or whether they each broke a leg together, has
not yet been clearly explained to us. Anyway,
Miln enjoyed a time worthy of his talents.
The students of Christchurch University threw
bunches of flowers at him, and the “ old Bluecoat-
boys ” of Auckland put their photos, into an album
and requested the tragedian to wear it next his
heart. He gave a lecture for the Ladies’, Bene-
volent Society, and accomplished other good deeds
too numerous to mention. Like a giant refreshed
he is now engaged in carving the name of Miln
deep in the memory of Wagga Wagga, and by
Tuesday next his classical standard will be proudly
floating in the breeze of Ballarat.
*& * *
A certain Lady Violet Greville —dealing
with that musty chestnut, the “degeneracy” of
modern drama—attributes the alleged falling off
in stage-work to the fact that “the stalls don’t
want to think, while the pit and gallery are in-
capable of it” —the noble lady intending “it ” to
represent “thinking.” Lady Violet’s belief in
the ability of chappies and their richly-upholstered
womankind to think, if they so desired, is not
more characteristic of her caste than is the sup-
position that people paying less than ten shillings
for a seat have no brains. The dear little idiot
has forgotten Garrick’s boast that the pit (corres-
ponding withjstalls in Australia) “rose at him.”
Now, whoever heard of the English stalls rising
at anything except “God Save the Queen,” or
legs ?
** » *
Gladstone went recently to the Hay market
Theatre to hear a Mrs. Erving Winslow, of Phi la'
delphia, read Ibsen’s “The Enemy of Society.’
Before the lady had finished Act 11. the Grand
Old Man was discovered to be asleep, and Mrs.
W.E.G. mercifully took him away while there
were still two and a half acts to be put through.
The Referee suggests that it was fortunate he
didn’t stay to hear Ibsen set forth the “moral ”of
his cranky sermon, viz.; “It is the compact
Liberal majority which is the greatest enemy of
society.” Ibsen’s political theory seems to main-
tain that a country must burst up directly the
masses are sufficiently enlightened to vote solid in
support of cardinal Republican principles. The
Referee speaks highly of Mrs. Winslow as a
reader, and expresses the hope that she will turn
her attention to “ some greater and more dramatic
dramatist” than the new Norwegian fetish.
That Ibsen is an undramatic writer will not be
generally admitted, however. He may, or may
not, be an exalted prig, treating of woman’s love
without having enjoyed the love of enough women
to become an authority on the subject, and he may
be given to suppose that no other man save Ibsen
comprehends the blessing of a good drainage
system (about which he has written a play) simply
because the common or garden kind of Norwegian
is accustomed to stinks. But as a play-writer he
is essentially tricky. It would be hard for anyone
else to arrange the few incidents of “A Doll’s
House ” more effectively than the author—an old
expert in stage business —has done. Think how
he piles up the agony—hollow though it be—in
connection with Krogstadt’s epistle, after its
dramatic fall into the letter-box !

George C. Miln was selected to deliver the
funeral oration on murdered President Garfield in
Chicago; consequently, when he waxes eloquent
over the corpse of Csesar, the tragedian is in con-
stant danger of amazing the Roman mob with
references to the Amurrikan Eagle and their Star-
spangled Banner. But probably they would not
be amazed. It is a question whether Caesar’s
excited supernumerary subjects would turn a hair
if Miln were to request ’em to “gaze upon the cold
clay of the President of our Great Republic,” or
“ see the rent that envious Guiteau made.”
** * *
At the Adelaide Royal “ The Mikado,”
by W. and G.’s Comic Opera Company, attracted
immense crowds for three nights, and would have
run many nights longer had not the management
been anxious to exhaust their repertoire during
the short season. Clara Merivale was a charming
Yum Yum, and, except when called on to act,
Imano was a most acceptable Pooh Bah. Mr.
Rosevear was an admirable Mikado, and Mabel
Mackay, in the absence of Fanny Liddiard, was
astonishingly good as Katisha, while Vernon,
Leumane, Grundy, with Katie Potter and May
Pollard filled the remaining parts satisfactorily.
“Princess Ida,” with Elton as King Gama, was
not a great attraction, and “ The Pirates of
Penzance,” with “Charity Begins at Home” as
an after-piece, will be shoved on to bring matters
round.

Dear Bulletin,- I suppose you are very tired of Ibsen
literature by this time, but 1 hope all the same you can
spare a few lines for ray opinion. I think that Ibsen,
being an old bachelor, knows nothing of a mother’s love,
and therefore the “ Doll’s House” is unnatural and a libel
on motherhood. Fancy a mother leaving her little ones
because her ideal is shattered! Most women have to put
up with that. I have seen women bear the greatest
brutality, both mentally and bodily, and find it impossible
to leave their children, knowing well that they could not
themselves find them bread. I cannot believe that such a
woman as Nora ever existed except in a man’s imagina-
tion.—A. H,, 3rd August, 1890.
Helen Hart was down for a “ Gospel address” at Hay-
market Reserve, Ballarat, one Sunday, and having
announced her text—“ Blessed are the peacemakers ”
waded into salvation chin-high ; just then a dog in her
vicinity began to howl, and the lecturess, seizing an
umbrella from one of her scanty congregation, knocked
this rival orator out in one act, and then remarked, “if
mothers would treat their sons like that, there would be
fewer larrikins in Ballarat.” Whereupon her audience
laughed consumedly, and Helen in dudgeon exclaiming,
“ I’m not in the habit of being laughed at,” at once made
tracks, and left the now sore-headed dog in possession of
the field.
