An alleged comic opera, “ Populaire,” by tw’o Scotch
gentlemen named Moser and M’Lean, has been performed
at Bur wood, Sydney, by local amateurs. This opera,
which will one day become a classic, had once previously

Si dney, some years ago—when Lord Loftus, it is alleged,

little left to live for.

Substance and Shadow.
(A SONNET.)
They do but grope in learning’s pedant round
Who on the phantasies of sense bestow
An idol substance, bidding us bow low
Before those shades of being which are found,
Stirring ot still, on man’s brief trial-ground ;
As if such shapes and modes, which come and go,
Had aught of Truth or Life in their poor show,
To sway or judge, and skill to sain or wound.
Son of immortal seed, high-destined man !
Know thy dread gift—a creature, yet a cause :
Each mind is its own centre, and it draws 1
Home to itself, and moulds in its thought’s span,
All outward things, the vassals of its will,
Aided by Heaven, by earth unthwarted still.
JOHN HENRY, CARDINAL NEWMAN.

Should you read the article again, you will see that it
deals with certain premises of George’s in order to show
how the value of his own deductions may be impaired by
close reasoning from his own basis. That the imposition
of the Single-tax would cause a glut in the property
market and a consequent depreciation of rents, is only
supposititious. The over value of all property which people
then desired to rent would be also attachable by the owner,
and, as now, only land of little value, inherent Or ac-
quired, would be a drug. The worker, lacking instru-
ments of production, is little better off than the ignorant
aboriginal in the struggle with monopoly. Should he,
desiring to better his condition, borrow, then he is usury s
slave, no matter what the land system. The nationalisa-
tion of all vacant or unused land, for simplicity and
thoroughness, lays right over the Single-tax on all sides..

Marriage on Commission.
The Latest London Sensation.
London, 12th Ado., 1890.
Duncan editor of the London Matrimonial News, has
been sued by a lady for breach of promise of marriage,
the damages being laid at £25,000. The plaintiff in the
rase against Duncan is a granddaughter of Sir Francis
Charles Knowles, Bart., and is 21 years of age. Defendant
is a man of 64 years, and in the course of the trial some
•stounding revelations were made. Plaintiff deposed
that defendant had made an attempt to seduce her. He
Las been twice married, having seven children, beside six
children of illegitimate parentage. The verdict in the
case gives damages to Miss Knowles at £lO,OOO.—S. M.
Herald Cablegram.
The most astonishing thing which The Bulletin
perceives in this case is that any court of justice
should award a woman of 21 years £lO,OOO
damages because she had not succeeded in hav-
ing_ herself seduced—for that is the only term
which can property be applied to marriages
so far contravening the laws of Nature —by
a man old enough to be her grandfather.
The alleged attempted “seduction” (the word
being here used in its conventional sense) being
the plaintiff’s secret, her character could only have
Huffcred by her own disclosure of that secret. It
would, possibly, have been very proper to punish
the ancient rascal, Duncan, by a £lO,OOO fine, pay-
able into the State Treasury ; but to award £lO,OOO
damages to a young woman who sought to hand
"herself over to a man at least 43 years her senior
is a shocking perversion of every principle which
should regulate dealings between man and
woman, and affords a gigantic premium to the
Worst sort of conspiracy.
# # #
In view of this startling case, The
Bulletin's readers may be interested in an ac-
count of an interview which took place in London
about five years ago between the now notorious
Duncan and the present writer. The narrative
C ntains nothing but fact:
In the Strand, a thoroughfare in the heart of
London, miming from the Law Courts to Charing
Gross, and in which arc several theatres, many
newspaper establishments, and an endless variety
cf prosperous shops, are situated the offices of the
M atriMonial News, a small weekly eight-page
fa int, whose existence is announced to the stranger
in two ways. Firstly, there is a large display of
bills in the lower windows of a dingy building at
the corner of an off-street, and then there are
runners who flourish copies of the News aforesaid,
which, it appears from handbills, is “specialty
addressed to those who want a husband or a wife.”
Such being the case, jfcho newsboys have not the
least hesitation in enquiring of you point-blank
*' whether you want to marry ?” and they are very
frequently met with the facetious fiction, some-
times perhaps founded on fact, that the person
addressed has just been up to the adjacent Palace
of Justice, either to file a bill in divorce, or to
enter an appearance as co-respondent.
#■ # #
The conductor of the Matrimonial News
could hardly have chosen a less savory site for
bis Agapemone, for what King-street is to Sydney,
the Strand is to London. Felise, Yolande, and
J uliette roll by in their landaus on their way from
theJPark, while the pallid Fragoletta and the dirty
Dolores wearily pace the footpath from mid-
day to midnight, plying their sad trade. The
same miserable spectacle confronts you, week in
week out —the bright eyes and the faded
dress, the bright dress and the faded .eyes, the
painted face, the dawdling walk, the “for God’s
Lake give me twopence to get a drink.” But,
saving the poverty, which in the Strand is to the
stranger even more shocking than the vice, matters
*ire pretty nearly the same all over the “civilised”
World: —
Her lips were red, her looks were free.
Her Tocks were yellow as gold,
Her face was white as leprosy,
The nightmare Life-in-Death was she
Who chills men’s blood with cold.
# # *
We tremulously enter the clingy building
with the much-billed window, which is, it turns
out, “only the publishing department,” and are
informed by the old man behind the counter —
who suggestively enough is, as we salute him, en-
gaged in mending a mouse-trap—that the private
office of the editor his master is at 76, further up
the street. We accordingly make our way to the
address named, and find that the same staircase
which leads to what, with necessary caution, is
merely indicated as “Editor's office, first floor,”
also brings one to the Adelphi Hotel, and to the
apartments of a teacher of singing—a truly de-
lightful emharras de choix —Woman, Wine, and
Song -
Who loves not woman, wine, and song,
Remains a fool his whole life long.
' ■ * # *
The clerks’ office—there are three clerks
•—also serves, we find, as the “gentleman’s wait-
ing-room.” The clerks are all old men—but we
don’t wonder much at the editor finding it neces-
sary to have in his service none but persons with
at least one leg in the grave. The eldest of the
trio has his trousers patched at the knees. We
don't marvel very much at that, either, after we
have gathered from a sotto-vocc remark made by
him to one of his fellow-toilers that he works
every night till twelve, and then walks all the
way to Islington. How much better off he would
be as a Murrumbidgee whaler ! There are in the
room three clients beside ourselves—a Jewish
gentleman with a squint; an individual with
ginger-curls, bearing the outward semblance
(qualified by a masher-collar as high as a sheep-
hurdle) of Christianity ; and an elegantly-attired
young gentleman, looking a little bit like George
Darrell and a good deal like the County Gentle-
man’s portrait of the Earl of Rocksavagc.
Perhaps he is an earl —you never can tell in Lon-
don. The last earl we saw—it was he of Cork,
■who, as “Master of the Baokhouuds,” or some-
thing of the sort, enjoys a fat sinecure—didn’t look
by any means too aristocratic to preside over his
absent assembly of w ell-bred dogs. Yes, perhaps
he is an earl. Perhaps, further, he is waiting to
arrange with the editor fur an elopement with
the daughter of a Duke. You have, doubtles.-,
at least heard of the latest good caricature—“ the
Masher’s Eiopement,” wherein the'girl stands at
the foot of the ladder at midnight ami tenderly
hands down the man of much collar.
* # #
For half-au-hour we sit in the clerks’ office
and glare at one another. The golden-haired man
glares most. The masher chews his stick, and
the Semitic squinter adds-up rows of figures in his
pocket-book. Meanwhile two of the clerks in-
dustriously enclose in envelopes a pile of letters,
evidently containing photographs, the third looks
dreamily outinto the dreary courtyard, and a cheer-
ful canary sings what is no doubt intended as the
epithalamium of a sheepish-looking couple who
have just come out of the editor’s room opposite.
Next to the editor's room is the “ ladies’ waiting-
room,” from which at intervals proceed faint
coughs, and arouhd which hCvers the attendant, a
venerable-looking crone of three-score-and-ten,
who surety will never, on the threshold of the
sanctum, snap away a client from her master.
Presently wo hear on the stairs the tread of fairy
feet, and one minute later there stand before us
two ladies who want husbands. One is a hatchet-
faced female of 36, in black-silk and tan-gloves.
She has prominent incisors and the eye of a
Marshal Soult. When she gets married again—
for she is surety a widow—she will always say, in
speaking on behalf of herself and her husband,
“I,” while he will always say “we.” She is
accompanied by a fat person who wears chocolate-
satin and a double-chin, and remarks that “it’s
very ot in the ladies’-room ” —which observation
is probably intended to explain and extenuate this
feminine invasion of the male sanctuary. Then the
axe-visaged woman expresses to her companion a
hope that “we’ll not have to wait again for an
hour and a quarter like we did yesterday.” It
occurs to one that, outside the office of the editor
of the Matrimonial News, she and her friend
might have to wait for husbands for a very much
longer period than an hour and a quarter. How-
ever, we don’t say so, but go on reading the paper.
# * ’ *•
At last the editor’s door opens to us, and
wo are in the presence of a tall, gaunt man, who
unites in his person several of the physical pecu-
liarities of Mr. Joseph Abbott, of N.S.W. political
celebrity, and > of Mr. M‘Kean, the Melbourne
solicitor. This is the editor. Wo have often
passed him in Fleet-street and speculated as to his
occupation, which we judged to bo of a scholastic
character. Ho is more than six feet high, and,
though evidently over GO years of age, his hair has
only commenced to be tinged with what is known
to the Australian aboriginal as “flour-bag come
up.” He has a saw-sharpening voice, like that of
Gormly, M.P., of Wagga; he indulges in George
Dibbs’ trick of at one moment dropping his spec-
tacles on to the tip of his nose and at the next
ORNAMENTAL—NOT USEFUL.
DRILL-INSTRUCTOR: “DIDN’T YOU HEAR MY ORDER?-WHAT ARE YOUR
EARS FOR?”
RECRUIT: “TO KEEP MY CAP FROM SLIPPING OFF.”
elevating them to the roof of his head, and he
wears his long hair in a semi-curled, half-wavy
condition, a little after the manner of the played-
out “Nunawading Messiah.” He went straight
to business, and in a) loud voice, which we were
dreadfully afraid might be heard outside, enquired
abruptly, but with a merry twinkle in his eye ;
“ Well, do you want to be married ?”
We entreated him to sit down and bo calm. He
began to laugh with a laugh as loud as that of Bob
Dodson. ** It was a serious thing to get married —a
still more serious*thing not to get married, ” We
had heard that remark before. Then we enquired
“ Are you married ?”
Once more he laughed consumedly, and while
he did so we took stock of a big box on his table,
labelled, “ Braden’s Prepared Groats.” It was
full of, photographs. A prosaic, but withal a
domestic-looking receptacle for the shadows of
beauty in nitrate of silver.
“No,” he mirthfully replied. “I am not mar-
ried ; I am a bachelor, and shall no doubt die a
bachelor. ”
“Benedick,” we remarked, “hoped to die a
bachelor, and yet lived to see himself married.”
“Ah,” said the editor, “ I see too many ladies,
to fall in love with any of them. The man who
speaks to 40 women a day is always a safe man ;
he who only talks with a woman once a week or
once a month, goes off just like that ” —and he
snapped his fingers and gave a Ifmd “ click.”
We explained that it was not our intention to
“go off like that.” We were a newspaperman,
and had come to ask the favour of an interview.
He lowered his glasses and looked at us fixedly.
Yes, we wished to tell Australia all about the
editor of the Matrimonial News. It would be a
good advertisement for him.
He took the idea and after a while commenced
to talk. “I have been 3G years in business, and
I was in business 28 years before I started my
paper. I began in 1848, and since then tens of
thousands of people have been married through
me.”
“ Any in Australia?” we enquired.
“Yes; lots married in Australia. Several in
Sydney, including a member of Parliament, whom
I married in 1861. He is dead now, I think.”
“ Very probably,” we muttered.
“Oh, you needn’t put it in that way,” replied
the editor. “My marriages all turn out well.
Here (picking up the photo, of a determined-
looking woman with high-cheek bones and banged
hair) is a lady I married five years ago. Her
husband is dead, and now she has come back to
me for another. ”
“ Do you call that turning out well ? ’
“It shows, at any rate,’ replied the editor,
“ that she is satisfied-; —”
“ And what about him ?” we asked. )}
“ He doesn’t complain, poor fellow, at any rate,
observed the editor, with a chuckle. We had
heard that.joke before, also. “Here is a man
from New Guinea ”
[So it was—our old friend G .]
“ Well, he wants a wife. I have always on my
books six or eight thousand people.”
Then he took up another photograph. “ There
is a lovely girl. Now, isn’t she?”
It was a coloured picture, and she wore in her
hat the wing of a bird of paradise. She was
nine-and-twenty at least, and square-jawed. We
shook our head.
“ Well, some like her, others don’t. Here,
again, is the photo, of a girl I married seven years
ago. She went to Brazil. This is a cousin of the
Marquis of Huntly—l married her, too. Here is
a lady with £50,000” (she needed gilding)—“l
got her a husband the other day. Her sister mar-
ried Mr. Walter , of Australia, a man with
£70,000. Just look at this handsome woman,
under 40. She is anxious to marry again, or if
you like you can have her daughter, a pretty girl,
only 19. Yes, they all come to me, daughters and
mothers —I see 80 people a day.
We asked him which were the more numerous
—his male or his female clients.
“I have,”returned ho, “rather more men than
women. That, I think, arises from the men get-
ting hold of my paper very extensively. Here,”
lie continued, “ is the kind of man I often have
lie is a magistrate of the county of Somerset. I
married Hobart Pasha
“And the Baroness Burdett-Coutts ? ”
“No, lam telling you the truth,” he replied,
somewhat severely. ‘ ‘ Here is the Widow of a late
Chief Justice of Natal. She wants a husband.
Here is a clergyman ” (a bald-headed man with .a
monkey beard) —“I have great numbers of the
clergy. Here is a lovely French lady—how do
you like her? She is 28, and has one little girl
four years old. This is a surgeon-major, a country-
man of mine, from Inverness. He has just gone
out to India after signing an agreement with me.
lam to give him a wife with £20,000. Here is a
lady ” (we know her well) “ who is married to Mr.
J of Queensland. I have married her twice.
I have had lots from Sydney. Look at this. ”
And he showed us the photos, of two ladies as
well known in Sydney as the face of John Davies,
Esq., O.M.G.
“Now,” he went on, “ you see the photo, of a
pretty girl with £SOOO. ”
“ Why should she come to you for a husband ?”
“ I don’t know. The wide and immediate
choice I am able to offer seems to have a fascina-
tion for women. If you like, you can marry her
mother.”
“ Thank you, very much,” we humbly replied.
‘ ‘ Here is a lady whose father is High Sheriff of
the county of .”
“You say,” interrupted we, “that you see 80
people a day ?”
“ Yes, and I spend £SO a week in advertising,
outside my own paper. I work till one o’clock
every morning—10 hours a day, and only take one
meal, breakfast, if you overlook the fact that
before leaving the office I have a glass of wine and
a biscuit.”
Yes, it was quite plain—the old boy lived on
love.
“ Just look at that beautiful girl ! Comes to
me fiom Grosvenor-square. Did you ever see
such lovely hair ? Here is a solicitor who comes
to mo for the third time for a wife. I married
him in ’53, and again in ’63, and now, at the age
of 64, he asks for my help once more. That is an
absolute fact. His eldest son is a clergyman, 28
years of age, and I found a wife for him, too ; one
with money. Here is a lady, aged 45, who is
going to be married on the 15th of July. She has
£4OOO, and writes me a note. Listen :‘ My dear
sr, now that I am engaged to be married, do not
give my address to anyone else.’ Here, again, is
a clergyman, the son of a bishop, from whom I
had £IOOO. I married him to a lady with over
£20,000. You see, I charge 5 per cent, on the
lady’s property.”
A flood of light here burst in upon us. “You
don't take commission from both sides, do you?”
“ Oh, no; the party with most money pays as a
rule. Of course. I get my fixed advertised fees in
each case.”
It struck us that if both sides could be operated
on simultaneously, aN.S.W. run-appraiser’s billet
would be a mere circumstance alongside that of
the editor of the Matrimonial News.
“Let me show you a copy of the London Daily
Telegraph, containing an article headed ‘ \
Seen#: at a Wedding ’—in 1882, it was. A client
of mine, a Sheffield solicitor, was going to marry a
handsome widow I had found for him, with
£28,000. The parties were at the altar when the
bride’s brother came and loudly objected th e
wedding.”
“ On what ground ?”
“ Well, that was what the clergyman asked.
The lady’s brother said she was mad, and that her
sister had been in an asylum, whereupon the bride,
groom suggested that, as there appeared to be
madness in the family, perhaps the brother was
mad too. Which was a clincher, especially as my
client pulled out of his pocket a certificate he had
got from two leading doctors, testifying that his
intended wife was perfectly sane. So the wedding
proceeded. An account of it was in every paper
in the United Kingdom, and it brought me thou-
sands of customers ; it added £IOO a week to my
income for three months, for every man in Eng-
land seemed to think I could get him a wife with
£28,000.”
The old man now laughed till he had to hold his
sides.
“ It did me a lot of good,” he continued, “ and
was the best advertisement I ever had, notwith-
standing that in my life I’ve spent £50,000 in
advertising. Even the bridegroom’s sister came
to me for a husband a week afterwards.”
“What percentage of the applicants do yon
many? ”
“Well, I should say about 15 to 20 per cent.
There are always a lot of people you can’t marry
anyhow. Hundreds of the men cannot, it is plain
to me, keep a wife, and so I send them off about
their business. Sometimes you get an old woman
you can’t dispose of. But plenty of women with-
out a penny make splendid marriages—in fact, if
a woman is at all good-looking she usually comes
off best in the long run, so far as my office is con-
cerned, though I very frequently find a rich hus-
band for a woman with money.”
Here he turned up the loaves of one of a large
number of huge volumes bound in parchment, and
showed us a list of entries : £385 commission from
one man, £lO5 from another, £O3O from a third—”
“That is a New Zealand man !” we exclaimed.
“Yes, one of the richest wheat-growers and
sheep-farmers in Canterbury. I married him,
you see, six years ago. This is the photo, of the
step-daughter of a general officer, who comes to
me nearly every week, but he wants the young
lady—who, though very accomplished and rather
handsome, has no money—to marry a wealthy
man. Plenty of fathers and mothers consult me
as to marrying their children. Here is the widow
of a baronet ”
“ Very old,” we muttered.
“She isn’t very old,” replied the editor, wiping
his spectacles. “You see, she has £SOOO a year.
Plenty of people at GO get married like shots.' The
other day I married a lady of 22 to a man of 70
who settled £20,000 on her. One old lady of 68
came to me not long since and said : ‘ Yon have now-
had my application for two years. Are you not
going to get me married before you die? You
know you might die before I get a husband, and
then whatever should I do? She evidently didn’t
care what happened to me so long as I fixed her
up
* * #
But perhaps you’re tired, of this kind of
thing. We have seen men die of snakebite, and
we have seen a man stand in the witness-box for
three hours in a cold sweat and hear his beery
love-letters read out to a cruel world. Yet after
all we are of opinion that you can wish your bit-
terest enemy no greater curse than that he should be
married by the editor of the Matrimonial News
either to the fat woman with the seamy chin or
to the skinny hag with the reaping-hook nose—or
for the matter of that to any one or more of the
hundreds of dismal, leering creatures whose dirty,
much-fingered photographs are stacked in the
editorial groat-box.
Victorian Reporters’ Association has
adopted a badge of membership—a silver medid
bearing the name of the association and the word
“ Press ” in blue enamel. The badge is intended
as a speedy passport on public occasions. How-
ever, the most efficacious passport has ever been
silver without any blue enamel.
At a Melbourne shop-door hung until
lately a picture of the British “ Royal Family,” to
which was appended a placard bearing this legend
—“Meeting of the Unemployed.” But a seedy
swell came along who evidently knew that it was
raining in Lending, for his threadbare pants were
neatly rolled back from his battered boots. He
saw the placard, he read the inscription, and then
saying “ Ha ! doncherknow” to himself three
times, tore the offending cardboard into frag-
ments, on which he trampled violently ere walking
off with a virtuous look, mortally afraid the shop-
keeper was coming out.
About the maddest man on record is a
lawyer, who recovered costs in the District Court
last week. The judge awarded him 10s. in addition
to the verdict, but he stopped to argue the point,
and, after twenty minutes’discussion, he sud-
ceeded in getting £2 10s. allowed for legal charges.
Then he turned round to collect the amount from
his enemy, and found the defendant's attorney
sitting behind him with an expression of blissful
contentment on his face, while the defendant him-
self had vanished. He had fled through a side-
door the instant the verdict was retarded against
him, and no one in these parts has seen him since.
Cha’u.emont and Co., high art photographers,
Royal Arcade, altogether owe their measure of
success to the fact that the merits of their photo-
graphy are always of a high order.

Personal Items.
Patchett Martin is at work on a life of Bob
Lowe.
Cardinal Moran every day takes at lunch
a stone buttle of ginger-beer.
Hubert Louis Stevenson has written two
plays during Ins brief absence from Sydney.
Jimmy Whistler, artist, has married the
buxom widow of Godwin, architect, one of whose
wives was Ellen Terry. Mixed —give it up.
The present Victorian Chief Secretary,
who is a spiritualist, is alleged to have stated that
Christianity was “merely a mental disease.”
The Trinity College (Melb.) students say
of Warden Leeper, whose effigy they recently
burnt, that he is more of a warder than a warden.
When Oolenso was inhibited from preach-
ing ip the English Cathedrals, he said : " Well,
well, t could go outside, if 1 liked, into Gods
Cathedral.”
J. F. Hogan, author of “ Tiro Australian
in London” and other works, has just published a
book named “ The Lost Explorer,” which tells the
story of Leichhardt.
It is said that in the new editions of
Dilke’s “ Greater Britain ” Parke,s will, in defer-
ence to influential representations, be given a pair
of wings and a halo.
Prof. Jenks is a small edition of Oscar
Wilde, before the latter cut his hair. Jenks’
matter is always good, but h_j manner is odiously
sing-song and jerky.
Queen Victoria while travelling, now
assumes the title of “The Lady Justice Mercy.”
Why not “Charity?” Possibly because Charity
usually remains at home. '
Cecil Tcece, barrister, the new M.L.A. for
Goulburn (N.S.W.), says ho “means to follow
politics as a profession.” This will give a splendid
chance to the rery junior bar.
Bishop Mporhouso lias been throwing in
a counterbalance to the worship of Miss Fawcett,
senior wrangler de jure. He would much prefer a,
woman who could cook his dinner.
Up to the present every Australian explorer
has been a pobr man. John Forrest (now West Aus-
tralian Minister of Lands) is the prosperous excep-
tion. He has made himself a millionaire.
Pierre Douar, the Pentridge escapee and
suicide was not, as lias been stated, a New Cale-
donian convict, but was a soldier in New Cale-
donia, where he deserted ; he had served with
bravery in Algeria.
The doctors have ordered Louise Michel
to be set at liberty, as her aberration is not dan-
gerous, but it is arranged for her to live at a poor-
house, with complete freedom to go in or out. The
public is very sensitive about tier.
Purves has got his hair cut short. He
took this precaution so that the next time Gaunson
jumped at him he might not be able to get a hold ;
but as David will probably clutch him by the ear
instead, the advantage doesn’t amount to much.
A Melbourne paper says that Victor J.
Daley is writing a book of fiction to be published
in London. Mr. Daley, who is one of the few
Australian writers who are, justified in styling
their verse poetry, is doing nothing of the kind,
so far as fiction is concerned at any rate.
The cable states that Mrs. O’Shea’s solici-
tors have thrown up the task of defending her
against the charges of adultery in which she is
joined with Parnell. However, as the cable-man
is the most mendacious of lying Tories, all infer-
ences from this statement may fairly be post-
poned.
Labouchere, speaking of John Morley as a
politician, regretfully says : “ The trouble with
these atheists is that they are too dashed scru-
pulous for us Christians.” If Labouchere is a
scrupulous Christian, then what, in the name of
Goliath and the other apostles, is an ordinary,
unprincipled Christian like ?
A certain English lord, who had more
daughters than any other member of the aris-
tocracy, walked out one day with six of his
daughters. Some one who saw him, remarked to
a companion, “Poor man!” The lord overheard
the observation, and turning to the person who
made it, replied, “ Not so ‘ poor ’ as you think ; I
have six more at home ! ”
Spagnoletti : “I heard Ned Kelly, in the
dock, say to Sir Redmond Barry, ‘Your Honor, we
will meet again sooner than you expect ’ —those
were the words, according to my recollection,
spoken respectfully, and without menace. There
was much speculation as to what he meant, and
people thought it prophesied a break out of gaol,
and visit to Sir Redmond’s. Everyone in court
heard this distinct saying of Kelly’s.”
Cardinals Newman and Manning were not
friends, and it was remarked that they never once
spoke while walking side by side in a sacred pro-
cession a year or two ago. The coolness dated
from a long time back, though they were, to some
extent, engaged together in the Tractarian move-
ment. Manning is a practical man, Newman was
not, and Pius IX never believed in him, but Leo
XIII did, wherefore the Cardinal’s hat came
along at once.
The Premier Permanent case was beautified
and adorned by a touching incident last week,
when one of the barristers engaged was so much
overcome by the thought of his client’s spotless
character that he had to adjourn his speech for a
few minutes and weep. When a lawyer has to
stop so that he may wail and splutter, the pressure
of pathos to the square inch is something tremen-
dous, and as his “refresher ” was going on all the
time, the incident was satisfactory to all parties
concerned.
Towards the close of the last French Em-
pire, Eug nie used to affect the utmost concern
about the hereditary and other antecedents of
ladies about to be presented at Court. She was
apprised one day of the marriage of a certain
Spanish Duke, an ex-assistant to a pharmaceutical
chemist, whom, for peace’ sake, Queen Isabella
had ennobled, and was asked to receive the lady.
“Who is this Duchess?” said Eugenie, to her
former liege lady. “There’s nothing to be ob-
jected to in her,” said Isabella. She’s rich, and i
hear that she’s the Pope’s daughter. One can’t
go higher than that.”
Hans Brandt, apprentice on a ship at
Hartlepool, fell down the hold lately and was
killed, when they found him to be a girl; which
revives a multitude of such cases. The most
remarkable is that of one Dr. Barry, quite an
eminent regimental surgeon at the Cape of Good
Hope, early in this century. He, that is to say
she, actually challenged an officer for calling her a
woman, but not till her death, well over middle
age, was the deception exposed. One such
deceiver had the front to marry a woman, and
they ultimately lived happily. This was at Ben-
digo, where the female-husband was a miner.
A Brighton (Vic.) bailiff, placed in posses-
sion of a house, in the process of recovering £6
10s., pulled to pieces the hud on which lay the
debtor’s wife, who f ell to the floor and prematurely
gave birth to a child. She sued the bailiff for
assault, but his defence that “he had a perfectly
legal right to seize the bed,” was upheld by a
bench of J VP.
A big Sydney syndicate has this week
started to bore for coal at Cremorue, Sydney,
about a couple of minutes’ distance from the chief
city wharf. A consensus of scientific opinions
points to the probability of coal being struck at
2700 feet, but failing such good fortune the bore
will if necessary be driven another thousand feet.
To work coal deposits at over 3000 feet seems
rather a large order, but the men interested are
quite confident that there is a big fortune to be
gpt out of operating on such far-down seams right
alongside deep wafer and within a stone-throw of
the city. They are so sure about the matter that
they have already sent to England specifications
for an enormously-]»owerful plant to be put in
hand on receipt of cable. They expect to start
their shaft within nine months from now.
The glove mania is spreading in Sydney.
In a swell locality may be seen any morning a
swell shoemaker, sweeping the pavement in front
of his shop with his hands encased in tight-fitting
kid gloves of the most expensive build.
At Brunswick (Vic.), a week or two ago,
a person named Males was fined £5 for selling a
bunch of carrots on a Sunday. As he hadn’t that
amount in the world he went to prison as the only
alternative, and left his wife, who was an invalid
and penniless, to live on raw turnips and the rest
of the stock-in-trade till he came out. Also about
the same time a man who bashed a stranger in the
street and tried to jump on his chest with both
feet, was charged 10s. ; and a person who was
fighting drunk and nearly wore out four police-
men, received a severe,caution. The law is not an
ass. It is a hytena.
One of the ruined colonists from the Ker-
madec Islands has petitioned N.Z. Parliament for
£B5O compensation, on the ground that the
Government had induced him by false representa-
tions to take up a run in that land of desolation
and slush and deluge. There was nothing meek or
humble in the petition, though, and he didn’t
wind-up by stating that ‘‘ he would ever pray.
There was an air of frank, ( unvarnished cursing
and dynamite in it instead;' and a sort of under-
lying insinuation that if the money wasn’t paid
someone would find himself lying dead at an early
date ; and the petitioner briefly intimated that if
the House didn’t settle with exceedingly great
velocity he would make the bill larger, and do
something that would lead the Assembly to wish
it had never been born.
There are 21,000 parsons in England. Of
these, over 3000 have stipends lower in value than
£l5O yearly, and a movement is on foot to raise
money by subscription for the augmentation of
their incomes. Such a list ought to be carried
round only among the 18,000 who are more highly
paid, for the British public spends quite enough
church-money as it is. Surely the 34 bishops
who among them, beginning with Canterbury at
£15,000 and ending with Truro at £3OOO, earn, or.
rather grab, £lOB,OOO yearly, thus dragging in the
fishes and loaves (especially the loaf) at an average
rate of nearly £5OOO each-would be willing to do
something for the poor curates with lean wives,
threadbare clothes, and big families'. Should they
belie their profession as Christians by refusal, then
the law should compel them to relax their grip on
the shekels by a readjustment of church salaries.

The Tasmanian Freetrade papers are fast
losing ground.
Bishop Moorhouse : “There are no leaders
in England now, only followers.
Now, watch England and Germany organ-
ising, their African Sepoys, and teaching the humble
nigger bayonet-exercise.
No politician who voted for a Chinese
Restriction Bill can afford to howl about the
iniquity of the “ boycott.
The present N.S.W. Government should
be known as the Pop-shop Ministry. They are
living on the interest of their pledges.
The North is represented in Queensland
Parliament by 16 members. Out of this number
the Separationist party reckon on securing at
least 12 or 13 seats.
The rebel Grenadiers are sent to the
Bermudas, but a batch of loyal ones is sent also,
to keep them from vnitrefaction ; whether the man
is loyal or not he goes to Bermuda.
Rev. Alex. Marshall, Melbourne Scots’
Church, says that “State secular education is
helping to build up an Australia of educated and
unprincipled scoundrels, the most dangerous class
in any community.”
Seven legal members -of the N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council voted against Attorney Trickett’s

Bill to amalgamate the profession. Any measure
which is voted against by seven lawyers must be
tolerably democratic.
Those N.S.W. legislators who are so fond
of boasting that they have “risen from the people”
should more soberly comport themselves. Other-
wise, it may be said of them that they have de-
scended from the people.
Addressing a meeting in London, says the
cable, Henry George said that the question of
Imperial Federation was out of the thoughts of
Australians. Their loyalty would not be worth
sixpence if subjected to any severe strain.
When boy-politician Caulfield was a pro-
tectionist the Sydney daily press declared that
his political speeches were “tirades of rubbish.”
Now that ho is a Foreign-trader he makes
“eloquent speeches’"and “ vigorously denounces ”
tnings.
Apropos of the proposal to allow Socialist
Rosa to address the Australian Natives, Purves,
Q.C., says, that while every latitude is to be given,
“the line must be drawn somewhere !” Why not
draw it at rich lawyers who refuse to hear the
other side? *
If a trades-union consisting of two people
of one trade is legal, then a trades-union consist-
ing of 100 people, or 50,000 people, of 100 different
trades is also legal. To logically dispute the
right of all trades to combine, Capital must begin
by denying the right of the members of any one
trade to combine.
When questioned, in the Lords, on the
Grenadier Guards’ strike, Premier Salisbury said :
“It is absurd to attach any significance to the
matter.” Just the same was said at the outbreak
of the American Civil War, of the French Revolu-
tion, of the U.S. War of Independence—and
everything else. Even the Australian Republic.
By 37 votes to 11 votes, the Members of
the N.Z. House of Representatives has affirmed
the principle of woman-suffrage. This, if imitated
by the other Parliaments of A ustralasia and carried
from the bare affirmation stage into afi'Act, would
soon settle various questions that now hang up,
like cocoons suspended in the breeze. The
“female compositor ” question, for instance.
It is all important to have Labour repre-
sented, in every Australian Parliament, at the
present juncture. In Victoria, the Conservative
Press Is always referring to Trenwith at? the repre
sentative of labour, with a kindly assumption thai
there ought to be just one representative in Barba
ment of two-thirds of the population. Trenwith
is a fine fellow, but not a labour man. Who la .
Unless the N.S.W. Opposition plays a
better game it will get Parliament dissolved before
the crafty Parkes has published his financial
policy—in other words, it will find itself before
the country with nothing to attack. Then Parkes
will get a big majority on general principles, in-
cluding the Yellow Pup, and will return to the
House and do just as he pleases for three years
more.
Salisbury’s answer in the Lords about the
Guards’ mutiny was as audacious as that of Cross
in the Commons, when he denied the accuracy of
the disclosure made beforehand, by Marvin, of
the Berlin Treaty. This took the gilt off the
gingerbread, by proving that Beaconsfield’s magni-
ficent Berlin triumph, “ Peace with Honour, was
settled in advance at London, with the Russian
ambassador.
No bowels of compassion have the banks,
some of whom appear to regard with the cold and
cruel eye of cynical indifference the prospect of a
death-grapple between the shearer and the
squatter. One or two of these institutions have
already informed squatters on whose stations
labour troubles have arisen that they will tolerate
no delay in the delivery of the wool over which
liens have been taken.
In the Victorian Assembly last week a wag
attempted to make Duncan Gillies blush by ask-
ing him whether he intended to question an Agk
statement that “ the object of the Government is
to retain its position by indirectly bribing
members of Parliament by bribing their con-
stituencies.” Duncan’s impenetrable hide main-
tained its normal leathern lint as he haughtily
replied—“ Too thin.”
One M‘Masters, writing in Melbourne
under the signature of f< Viator,” is the only pen-
man of the dailies who does justice to the Wharl
Sunday meetings, and, as usual, his articles appeal
in the most unlikely quarter. How ridiculous non
appear the, paltry efforts of the Harbor Trust to
stop these wharf meetings, which were thus only
advertised, and made more powerful engines for
the political education of the masses !
The Novoe Vremva, one of the foremost
Russian papers, reviews 1 Hike’s “Greaterßritain,
especially the part bearing on Russia in Asia, and
writes that the Russians will storm Herat soon.
The left arm reaches for Herat, _ thfv right _ for
Constantinople, and both work in harmonious
sympathy. England works up to Candahar, but
under tremendous engineering difficulties, as com-
pared with the easy Russian trip to Herat.
H. M. Stanley, freebooter, land-pirate,
and nigger-butcher in the name of God, continues
his denunciations of tho Germans for what he
calls their “monopolisinggreediness.” This im-
pudent brigand, whose best sponsor was a flour-
sack, quite loses sight of the fact that in de-
nouncing the Germans he emulates the vulture
who “denounced” the other vulture for picking
out the eyes of the traveller before he was dead.
The cry among some of the marine officers
who deserted their Association at the first scare
was, and is, well represented by the following
quotation from a letter published in the Argus
“ Surely the marine officers do not want to descend
in the social scale, and lower their status, which
they assuredly would do by allying themselves
with the labour organisations.” It is enough to
make philosophy’s self commit suicide to hear such
tommyrot talked in a country raised from nothing
—ay, from worse than nothing—raised from a
manufactured hell—and sustained by_ this very
labour which these porcelain dolts despise.
There is an unfinished law-suit on hand at
Adelaide. The Fire, Brigade there had a horse
which was too pensive and too much lost in
thought for galloping to conflagrations, so they
sold the animal on credit to an undertaker. Then
the beast brightened up and bolted with a car-
riage-load of bereaved mourners, and the under-
taker sent it back as being too irreverent for his
business, and the Fire Brigade sued him for
breach of contract. Unfortunately, however, the
fire-alarm rang just as the superintendent was
giving his evidence and he had to grasp his brass
helmet and run, and the position of that horse re-
mains an unsolved conundrum.
British justice is about as remarkable in
its way as the Australian article. A tattered
hawker was arrested some time ago in the streets
of London for having no visible means of support,
and a bank-book was found on him showing that
he had £2O to his credit. The book was promptly
confiscated, and Sir William Hardman sent him
up for six months. And now that his term has
expired the law has taken possession of his savings
to pay for his support in gaol during the period
which he spent there on a charge of having no
support at all, and when he wanted to know what
kind of a statute it' was which required him to
defray his board and lodging in person, and to
pay for being shut up and knocked about with a
bunch of keys, he was warned to clear out or
something worse might happen to him. Shades of
Scroggs and Jeffreys, where are you?
The Irish Monthly has a curious article
on the Mormons, by “ A Nun,” a counterblast to
that in the Atlantic Monthly in favour of
Polygamy, by Brigham Young’s daughter, Mrs.
Gates. The Nun reiterates the statement that
the Mormons never could get hold of Irish people,
and, of course, there is a beautiful, well-deserved
panegyric on the poor Gurrul of the Bogs in her
red cloak, as she takes off her shoes to walk to
Mass. We don’t know exactly how far the oft
repeated statement applies that the Mormons get
no Irish, but certainly the latter are remarkably
exceptional in the Mormon Rush. It is not that
“ No Irish Need Apply,” for the Long Hairs and
Beardies. are always struggling after them—so
says the “Nun ”in her own way. Disraeli
said, apropos of the Darwin Ape discussion, “ I
am on the side of the Angels.” So are we. The
little Nun is better than the blowsy Mrs. Gates.

Soci et y
The case of Florence Martin, convicted at
Sydney of bigamy and sentenced to but ten
minutes’ imprisonment, on account of the shocking
treatment to which she had been subjected by her
first husband, gives rise to an interesting question :
How does the unhappy woman now stand in rela-
tion to the divorce law? Having committed
bigamy, she cannot, wo presume, possibly get a
divorce from Mai tin, and any children she may
have by her present husband will thus be illegiti-
mate. Nor can Martin, after ill-treating his wife,
claim a divorce from her. How can Florence
Martin be placed in a position to free her from the
ban of the social law ?

tion cla,-s, and when a bad young man planks

* * #
Marine officers in the Australian coasting-
trade, unlike the sailors engaged therein, get no
overtime. The difference between the pay of
the third and fourth officers on one hand and
ordinary seamen on the other is at times
so great that the sailors’ wives could afford
to hire the officers’ wives to wash for them.
And all this for the glory of wearing brass
buttons ! The Bulletin does not for an instant
assert that the steamship-owners are making fat
dividends —on the contrary, it is perfectly aware
that steamship shares now generally mean some-
thing between 6 per cent, and insolvency. But
the fact remains that the men are well paid
because they have been able to combine, while
the officers are poorly paid because they have
had no effective backing from the iron hand of
Labour.
* * *
By securing the conviction for perjury of
M‘Minn, who imputed to him immoral conduct
with a lady-parishioner, Rev. G. H. Allnutt, of
Gobbi tty, N.S.W., has secured a complete and
effectual legal vindication of his character and that
of the lady maligned. The difficulties of obtain-
ing such a conviction are in all cases very great,
inasmuch as, notwithstanding what society may
say to the contrary, it is a cardinal rule among
manly men to commit perjury a thousand times
over rather than make any admission compromis-
ing a woman. When, therefore, a jury taking
into consideration, as they naturally would do,
the tendency of ordinary witnesses to withhold
the truth in such matters, find that M’Minu was
guilty of perjury, just as the jury in the civil case
for damages returned a similar verdict, it becomes
evident that Mr. Allnutt’s case must have been a
very convincing one indeed.

What sort of a funeral you get doesn’t
matter much, but there was a pathos about a poor
young sailor’s burial, in Williamstown, the other
day. He died on shipboard, and the captain came
ashore with the body, which he saw put in the
hearse, and then he returned to the ship again—
so that the young sailor went to the grave without
a single person following. Now if this was in a
novel, you would have the captain going home to
the poor lad’s mother- We did the best for him,
mann, and here’s a lock of his hair,” etc.

“Frou-Frou ” is a bitter satire on private
theatricals, for Valreas insinuates himself with
Frou-Frou through their rehearsing together. The
authors here touched a social sore. The average
man, such is the depravity of human nature, does
feel uncomfortable when ho sees his wife arfisti-
cally and platonically hauled about, and Valreas
is an awful scoundrel. Even if he were a Joseph,
it wouldn’t be nice.

Thus a Loudon paper :
A wealthy man appears in London, he knows no one, he
is the horny-handed son of toil, or the great farmer, or
the speculative entrepreneur. Howls his entrance into
the great world to be compassed ? It is easy enough.
London society, though monarchical in its government,
is republican in its views ; it asks no questions and is told
no lies ; it merely inquires if the aspirant to ts favours is
rich, and in that case the portals of its most secret
chambers fly open joyful y. One great lady takes the
new-comer in hand, issues invitations for a concert (as
being more uncommon than a ball), collects all her
friends, and the trick is done. Henceforth year by year
the millionaire’s position improves ; at first smart people
will only go to his balls and concerts, presently they con-
descend to accept dinners at his hands ; royalty next
expresses a wish to see a specimen of his famous cook's
performances; finally, the millionaire enters the precincts
of the royal palace ; then, indeed, the climax of his hap-
piness is reached. He may say with Sydney Smith’s
epicure, “ Fate cannot harm me,' I have dined to-day.”
All this is evidently for the benefit of Mr. Mount
Morgan Robertson.

A picnic was recently given to the choristers
of St. Peter’s Cathedral, Adelaide. The bishop
and the cathedral organist took the boys to Oak-
hank, where games were played in which Ids
lordship participated. As the party left the scene
of the picnic they passed through the yard c.f the
well-known .Oakbank brewery, and the foreman
courteously offered samples of beer to the adults.
“ G. W. Adelaide,” in thanking the foreman, said
it was really very good beer, whereupon the fore-
man asked him if he had never tasted it before.
The bishop said he had not to his knowledge.
“Go on!” said the foreman; “mean to say* you
never knock round the pubs, at night?” He did not
recognise the bishop, who was dressed in flannels.
A certain wealthy young squatter whose
family name is talismanic w th the gentle Aus-
tralian barmaid from Cape York right down to
Cape Otway, hJs a station in Western Victoria,
and at shearing-time three seasons ago he wanted
the men to work under station rules, which they
declined to do and threatened to strike work.
The manager, having informed Mr. C what
had taken place, the latter came to the shed and
ordered all the men off the boards, remarking,
“I’d like to know who the ringleader of this
adjective push is, because if he’s a man I’d bo
glad to settle it outside the shed with him.” One
Billy Bradshaw hereupon said he was the cuss
and was quite willing to square the dispute in the
good old fashion. The shearers all egged Brad-
shaw on to have a slap at the boss, and in due
course a ring was formed arid the squatter and
shearer stripped to the waist. After several rounds
had been fought, the former called time and threw
up the sponge, saying that Bradshaw was the bes
adjective man he had ever met, and the best thing
he and his mates could do was to start shearing
again, and he would give them sixpence a hundred
over union rules.

The gigantic Australia Hotel, now rapidly
approaching completion in Castlereagh-street,
Sydney, is to be taken o> er by a limited liability
company. Granted good management, the Aus-
tralia will shut up several of the tomb-like
caravanserais still furnished with the chairs of 20
years ago, and infested by the ghosts of dead-and-
gone squatters. Many unencumbered people
already think of abandoning housekeeping and
taking apartments at the big place, the turn-over
at which will require to be enormous, as the mere
item of rent alone riiust represent about £13,000 a
year.

A Sydney sectarian paper asserts that
“ the money-offerings in a religious service widely
differ from the money taken at public concerts,”
because “the collection is part of the service of
the sanctuary.” Yes, alas! the collection is
always part of the service. Anyone who has wit-
nessed a service of which a collection did not form
part will please communicate with this office at
once. .Reward.

It has frequently been remarked how
closely a husband and wife come to resemble each
other after a long wedded life. A similar remark
seems to apply to actors and actresses after a long
period of professional association. Look at Irving
and Ellen Terry, for instance; and look at the
portraits of Mrs. Brown-Potter and Kyrle Bellow
in the shop windows !

Queer scene in a Melbourne school. Master
called up little Jimmy Butterfield, before all the
boys and girls, and Jimmy thought he was going
to get a prize, when the grim pedagogue said,
“ I’m told you’re the boy that falls in love with
the girls. We won’t have that nonsense here, d’ye
mind?” Two Melbourne-boys, one 14 years old,
committed suicide for love. One, for the"sake of a
pantomime fairy, flung himself under a railway
train; the other, a 16-year-old, suicided over
Emelie Melville.

The awful position of John Northcote, a
married Melbourne builder who trifled with the
affections of a barmaid and found himself brought
up for “breach” last week, was briefly, bluntly
and solemnly depicted by Townshend M’Dermott,
defendant’s barrister-at-law. The amorous John
had “merely done what was unfortunately too
common ” —pleaded Mac., who is quite old enough
to pass a valuable opinion on such matters—“ and
would be sufficiently punished by the tongue of a
virtuous and I’espectable wife.”" The argument
went straight to the soul of every married man in
court. What heavier damages than the tongue of
chaste matronhood could any barmaid wish to
inflict upon her faithless John ? (She got £5O in
addition to the other compensation.—Ed. B.) —

“GILHOOLEY’S ESTATE.”
(A ballad concerning the amalgamation of the legal
professions.)
Oh, Mi - . Gilhooley he turned up his toes,
As most of us do, soon or late ;
And Jones was a lawyer, as everyone knows,
So they took him Gilhooley’s Estate.
Gilhooley in life had been living so free
’Twas thought his possess! ns were great,
So Jones, with a smile, says, “ There’s many a fee
For me in Gilhooley’s Estate.”
They made out a list of his property fine,
It totalled a thousand-and-eight;
But the debts were nine-hundred-and-ninety-and
nine—
The debts of Gilhooley's Estate.
So Mrs. Gilhooley says, “Jones, my dear man,
My childer have little to ait.:
Just keep the expenses as low as you can
Against poor Gilhooley’s Estate,
But Jones says, “ The will isn’t clear in its terms,
I fear it will need some debate,
And the law won't allow me (attorneys are worms)
To appear in Gilhooley’s Estate.”
So a barrister-man, with a wig on his head,
And a brief in his hand quite elate,
Went up to the Court where they bury the dead,
Just to move in Gilhooley’s Estate.
But his Honor the Judge said, “ I think that the
joint
Legatees must be called to \m\hotc —
Ex parte Pokehorney is clear on the point—
The point of Gilhooley’s Estate.
“ I order a suit to be brought just to try
If this is correct that I state —
A nice friendly suit, and the costs, bye-and-bye,
Must be borne by Gilhooley’s Estate.”
So Mrs. Gilhooley says, “Jones, you’ll appear !
Thim barristers’ fees is too great;
The suit is but friendly.” “ Attorneys, my dear,
Can’t be heard in Gilhooley’s Estate.”
From Barristers’ Court there’s a mighty hurrah
Arises both early and late :
It’s only the whoop of the Junior Bar
Dividing Gilhooley’s Estate.
THE BANJO.
VIRTUE'S VENGEANCE.
Poor builder, the advocate rightly
And pungently pleaded your case ;
Mo doubt Mrs. Northcote is nightly
Suffusing with blushes your face,
And slanging that barmaid until U !
’Neath blankets you bury your head,
And wake to discover the pillow
Quite wet with the tears you have shed.
The heart of the hub. who goes mashing,
Away from his home and his hearth,
Turns cold when the missis is flashing
Her eyes in connubial wrath ;
’Tis pleasant enough while you bluff her
With yarns Ananias would scorn,
But O ! when she “ tumbles ” you suffer,
And wish you had never been born.
Though sweet are the joys of Giovanni
(While still the “old woman” is blind)
As Hebe gives ear to his blarney,
And smileth the smile of her kind,
Yet bitter the gay dog’s repentance
When, sooner or later, his wife
Discovers his game, and the sentence
Is nag —for the rest of his life.
Poor builder, your advocate clearly
Explained the position of things ;
We pity your sorrows sincerely.
(Which “we” is the poet who sings.)
Alas ! foolish butterfly gadder,
More shrill than the note of a fife,
More sharp than the tooth of an adder
Is the tongue of a virtuous wife.
No Doll’s House, we guess, is your dwelling,
No Nora, you bet, will arrange
To fly from her husband and dwelling
Because she considers him “strange,”
Such wives don’t exist out of Norway.
Your bosom is bound to be wrung
By a matron who stands in the doorway
To give you the length of her tongue.
It ought to be plain to a jury,
And patent to judges as well,
That the taunt of a virtuous fury
Is ten degrees hotter than hell.
What odds to a golden-haired Tottie
Is the loss of a dissolute swain ?
If her rival is driving him “dotty ”
Miss Tot hasn’t suffered in vain.
A lecture you’d better prepare to
Sit out every morning and eve ;
Of the ills giddy husbands are heir to
There’s none to beat that , we believe.
You will deem the rash suicide happy
Whose end is a bullet or knife ;
Even Totth- will murmur, “ Poor chappie!”
As she thinks of the virtuous wife.

o * #
Mrs. Stanley, the wife of the retired
nrigand who has lived for so many years on hope
and sand in the Dark Continent, claims to be a
descendant of Oliver Cromwell. Considering
Oliver’s incurable snuffle, and the way lie began
by talking' about liberty and ended by pounding
everybody who wanted a share of the article, and
taking into account that he brought up his son on
such a strict moral basis that he became three
parts an idiot, and making due allowances for his
massacres in Ireland, and for a disagreeable habit
he got into of selling his prisoners as slaves on the
American plantations, and reckoning in several
other trifles, it seems as if Stanley had married
into a thoroughly congenial family, and ought to
feel himself quite at home.
* # #
A Sydney man, who has a pleasant little
habit of making free with strangers in public, re-
ceived the surprise of his life the other day when,
with his usual good taste, he attempted in a bar to
make a butt of an inoffensive musician, better
known for the length of his hair than the weight
of his fist. When the funny man had got his hat
into shape, clapped a beefsteak on his eye, and
wrung the blood out of his nose, he began to
think that there might be something in the fable
of Samson and his locks after all.

* * *
The fund raised in Sydney at Jubilee time
to commemorate the 50th year of the reign of the
well-gilded individual known in the selectest circles
as the “Pre-eminent in Grief,” was originally
christened “The Queen’s Fund.” However, it
soon became plain that for collecting purposes the
“Woman’s Fund” was an ever so much better
title, and so the “ Woman’s Fund ” it became
accordingly. Now that Lady Carington is going
away, and the institution is to be used for a big
and fashionable ball, it bobs up again serenely as
“The Queen’s Fund.”
* * #
The most fascinating girl-movement at
lawn tennis is a sort of back-kick in smacking at
the ball, when they hop up and gyrate, with a
fish-hook effect, in snaring the susceptible. This
kick has been fatal to many a fine young fellow
swathed in flannel. We believe it is professionally
termed the Gyroscopic Ankle. There is a nautcll-
iike twist of the whole body, a corkscrew wrench,
at which young Mr. Green, with £15,000 a year,
foolishly applauds.

Roundabout.
A social function took place the other day
at a far-western hamlet, and for the time being a
spirit of joyous harmony lighted up the dull path
of existence, and the cares attendant on the present
low price of bullocks were extinguished. The hall
was tastefully decorated with tallow candles, and
an ounce and a half of hot grease fell into the eye
of the lady whose husband fell out of his cart last
year and broke his neck. There were about 120
people present, including an individual who was
Out on bail on some absurd question relating to a
horse, and not forgetting a maiden six feet hi h
who wore about three-quarters of an emu in her
hat. This blushing creature was of a bashful
turn, and was with difficulty ejected from the
ladies' retiring-room, where she wanted to stay all
the evening; but she recovered her spirits later
on, and threw her feet about so energetically in
the first waltz that they arrived unexpectedly in
the back of anybody who happened to be
near. The only mishap of the even ng arose
through the fact that a girl from Simpson's
Crossing had brought a piece of a dismantled
cruet with her as a scent-bottle, She was a pro-
longed and shadowy creature, and was, unhappily,
in profound ignorance of the fact that someone
had filled up the bottle with ammonia, but when
she took a liberal sniff of the mixture the circum-
stance dawned on her. Then she opened her
mouth in agony till all that could be t-eeii of her
was a portentous cavity surrounded by an almost
imperceptible fringe of girl, and uttered a shriek
that sounded as if a fire bad broken out in per-
dition and all the devils were rusting for the door.
When she had partially reeove’ ed she commenced
to canter round the room at the rate of a long dis-
tance in a very short time, but she was captured
after a brief but exciting struggle and led out into
the open air, where some kindly friends stood her
up against the wall. Her partner suspected that
this outrage had been perpetrated by a gen-
tleman without a coat and with a badly-bayed
collar, and he accordingly knocked him backwards

* * *
Any person who tliirsts after knowledge
can be abundantly supplied at Orange (N.S.W.),
where about five tons of literature are lying in
the gutter. Some two years ago an affable hawk
visited the town to collect information for a work
on the greatest men in the whole earth, and it
proved on investigation that the population of
Orange consisted almost entirely of great men,
and every stupendous intellect in the place
arranged to have his biography inserted, and to
take one or more copies. There was a consider-
able sameness about these biographies, however,
and most of them consisted of a bald narrative to
the effect that Thomas Jones, of Dead Hor c
Creek, was the son of old Jones, of the same
place, and that up to the age of 40 years he had
shown no particular evidences of genius, and had
continued that way ever since. Also, he farmed
480 acres of arable land, and was the father of a
large, bare-footed girl through his marriage
with Mary Ann Smith. The biographer
tried his best to cast a glamour of romance
over these naked facts, but the scheme fell
through miserably, and now everybody de-
clines to accept or pay for the volume on the
greatest men in the whole earth, and the agent is
delivering it at the hazard of his life. Generally
he has to bang the stupendous literary effort down
on the verandah and then fly for his existence,
while the recipient rushes out and hurls the book
after him, and then pursues him with dreadful
execrations and a gun. The town of Orange is
filled with books and dogs and guns and profanity
and placarded all over with notices that book-
agents will have boiling-water thrown at them,
and be shot afterwards and buried in unconse-
orated ground, and there is consternation every-
where. But the one point on which everybody is
agreed is that the agent will have to climb over
the subscriber’s d ad body to get the money ; and
if he recovers a verdict in the local courts and all
appeals prove to be useless, then, as a last re-
source, Orange intends to hoist the flag of insur-
rection and throw up entrenchmen s and defend
its liberties with bricks and pit> liforks and garden
implements, and a provisional constitution will be
established, the'basis of which will be that the
new republic will swim in blood before it will pay
for that thrice-infernal volume either in this world
or in the next.

There is a live nobleman living in a humble
sort of way in Melbourne, and apparently he lias
been changing to new quarters lately, for a cartage
firm now publishes a standing advertisement to
the effect that “ Lord Blank desires to state that
he is extremely satisfied with the way his furniture
was removed, and the charges were very moderate.”
We. would point out that if his lordship had
shifted his own tilings unobtrusive’y in the dark
the charges would have been still more moderate,
and a good deal of invigorating exercise would
have been thrown in. The present writer adopted
that system frequently in his younger days, and,
except on one occasion, when he undertook to
move the stove a distance of three miles on hi.i
back, and get it up two flights of stairs, he never
was any the worse. Provided Lord Blank doesn’t
gallop recklessly down the strict wheeling two
bedsteads and a sofa in a barrow, and is careful
not to fall backwards when he is toiling up to the
third storey with the chest of d aw rs in his
arms and the wash-basin on his head, he will
lie all right ; also it is important that ho
shouldn’t j ump too enthusiastically along a crowded
thoroughfare when he has got his hands full of
buckets and things, and is carrying the garden
rake in his teeth, else lie may be surprised after a
while to find that he is carrying away an eye on
one of the prongs of the implement. He will
probably discover, by and bye, that there are 116
ways of fixing up a bedstead, and that they are
always wrong ways and provocative of strong
language and du-t, and he may be inclined to re-
pine when he is falling downhill with a feather-
bed and a tin of keresone, and knows that the
family is overtaking him rapidly accompanied by
the axe and the dust-box and the frying-pan, but
he should boar up against these difficulties and
overcome them. The writer of tins generally lets
Ids fat aunt get in front with the feather-bed.

A gigantic poorhouse, endowed by a local
resident was opened lately at Auckland (N.Z.),
amid much snuffle and congratulation. A parson
named Reed in particular was taken with a bad
fit of loyal rejoicing, and in the course of Ids re-
marks he said that “His Excellency must be
gratified to find that the institutions for which
the Home Country is so celebrated are taking
root here, and ho hoped they would flourish.”
Which means, if language amounts to anything,
that his Excellency is glad to see the grand old
workhouse taking root and wants the glorious old
pauper to flourish in the land because that shoeless
mendicant is one of the symbols of England’s
greatness, and is anxious that the parochial beadle
should increase and multiply, and become abund-
ant in the new countries beneath the Southern
Cross. Also ho yearns that the gruel and the
skilly may become a part of the national existence,
and may spread like a green bay-tree; and he
wants the casual ward to grow into a great in-
stitution in order that the toothless cripple may
always be able to break stones before breakfast,
and bless the squire and all Ins relations. And
then another parson rose and pointed out that
under the enlightened policy of the Christian
Anglo-Saxon there was always a mouldy crust
open to the aged, and a plank bed for the infirm,
and that the man who was overtaken by sickness or
calamity would find a partially decayed vegetable
at his disposal provided he grovelled in the proper
quarter. Some religions, he added, taught the
degraded heathen to assassinate the helpless and
the aged, and put them out of their misery, but
the civilised white man abhorred such tilings.
We are now looking for the tomb of the man who
founded that “ religion ” which suggests that it is
better to kill the old mendicant than to put him
in a poorhouse; find when we have found it we,
intend to go to the expense of a tombstone, with
an inscription setting forth that a square heathen
lies below, and that he was a merciful and upright
idolater, and an honourable pagan who loved his
fellow-creatures, and of such is the kingdom of
heaven. Further we will give that lost barbarian
a eulogistic notice in The Bulletin as soon as we
have traced his history; and if his descendants
can be found we would suggert that they receive
some mark of public esteem as being connected,
however remotely, with a noble philanthropist,
whose name we trust will be held in reverence for
ages after the last beadle and Board of Guardians,
are extinct, and the final benevolent committee
and charity organisation society have been thrown
into the sea.

The musical eveningat Government House,
Adelaide, to welcome Sir Charles and Lady Halle
was a great success, notwithstanding that the
music performed.was all by local composers.
A correspondent tells ns of a welcome
recently accorded to a new chum in Port Darwin.
Fifteen gentlemen assembled and had a “ jam-
boree.” The little party sat down at 0 p.m., and
separated exactly at midnight, having meanwhile
consumed 87 bottles of lage -beer and six bottles
of whisky. Anyone who is astonished at so small
a performance can never have visited a German
club.
A fat and semi-divorced opera-singer, who
wants to marry her business-manager, had a bad
experience recently in an Australian city. She
had the wedding-feast prepared, the guests in-
vited and the wedding-gown laid out, when—
behold ! there occurred a hitch in the divorce-
papers, and the whole entertainment fell through
with a hollow sound. The guests rose nobly to the
occasion, however, and wolfed the breakfast all
the samp.
We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the.
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want. *
A publican at Bacchus Marsh (Vic.) has
been fined because he refused to supply bed and
board at the customary rates to Miss H elen Hart,
a massive lady clamped with iron, who lectures
on the “Sphere of Woman.” Whether it was
Miss Hart’s appetite that scared him, or whether
it was the hostile expression of her / ye, the defen-
dant did not state, but apparently he felt it would
be cheaper to be fined than to h- ■ -the lady in the
house. Possibly he was righ . We travelled
once on board a Union steamer with a lady-
lecturer, and she was the most 'expensive
person who ever came on board. Her strong point
was a mysterious “sphere” which the soul was
alleged to enter by a Hidden Way, and when once
it got in the present became non-existent and the
past was obliterated, and the future assumed a
new aspect which was not very well-defined ; and
all ordinary mortal existence ceased, and indivi-
duality was extinguished, and the individual
who had passed the threshold became
filled with mystic gifts and cognisances, and
the price of tickets was 2s. fid. And in the
course of a six days’ voyage this ethereal creature
ate everything on board except the deck and the
mainmast, and manifested a complete acquaintance
with every Act of Parliament that was ever
passed under which a lone female could recover
damages on any earthly pretence whatever. But
at last when it became evident that between eat-
ing and actions for damages she was likelydo own
the whole ship within a fortnight the captain re-
solved to give her the slip, and he steamed out of
Wellington harbour an hour ahead of time, and
left her desolate on the shore. At least we all
supposed that she was left desolate, but she fol-
lowed in a special steamer, and overtook our
vessel at Gisborne, and charged the special
steamer to the company, and the latter proceed-
ings of that female were worse than the first.

INDIAN LONG ARM LOUNGES.
AND
An Imperial Rescript.*
Now this is the tale of the Council the German
Kaiser decreed,
To ease the strong of their burden, to help the
weak in their need.
He sent a word to the people, who struggle, and
pant, and sweat,
That the straw might be counted fairly and the
tally of bricks be set.
The Lords of their Hands assembled; from the
East and the West they drew —
Baltimore, Lille, and Essen, Brummagem, Clyde,
and Crewe.
And some were black from the furnace, and some
were brown from the soil,
And some were blue from the dye-vat; but all
were wearied of toil.
And the young King said, “I have found it, the
road to the rest ye seek :
The strong shall wait for the weary, the hale shall
halt for the weak ;
With the even tramp of an army where no man
breaks from the line,
Ye shall march to peace and plenty in the bond of
brotherhood —sign: ”
The paper lay on the table, the strong heads
bowed thereby,
And a wail went up from the people : “ Aye, sign-
give rest, for wc die ! ”
A hand was stretched to the goose-quill, a fist was
cramped to scrawl,
When—the laugh ot a blue-eyed maiden ran cleat
through the council-hall.
And each one heard Her laughing as each one saw
Her plain—
Saidie, Mirth, or Olga, Gretchen, or Mary Jane.
And the Spirit of Man that is in him to the light
of the vision woke ;
And the men drew back from the paper, as a
Yankee delegate spoke:
“ There’s a girl in Jersey City who works on the
telephone;
We’re going to hitch our horses and dig for a
h .use of our own,
With gas and water connections, and steam-heat
through to the top ;
And, W. Hoheuzollern, I guess I must work till I
drop.”
And an English delegate thundered : “ The weak
an’ the lame bo bio wed !
I’ve a berth in the Sou’-West work-shops, a home
in the Wandsworth Road ;
And, till the ’sociation has footed my buryin’ bill,
1 work for the kids an’ the missus. Pull up ? I’ll
be damned if I will!”
And over the German benches the bearded whisper
ran —
‘‘ Lager, der girls, und der dollars, dey makes ot
dey breaks a man.
If Schmitt haf collared der dollars, he collars dei
girl deremit;
But if Schmitt bust in der pizness, we collars dei
girl from Schmitt.”
They passed one resolution: “ Your sub-committee
believe
You can lighten the curse of Adam when you’ve
lightened the curse of Eve.
But till we arc built like angels, with hammer and
chisel and pen,
We will work for ourself and a woman, forever
and ever, amen.”
Now this is the tale of the Council the German
Kaiser held —
The day that they Razored the Grindstones, the
day that the Cat was Belled,
The day of the Figs from Thistles, the day of the
Twisted Sands,
The day when the laugh of a maiden made light of
the Lords of Their Hands.
—Rudyard Kipling,
*We print the above as a sample of good verse, not be-
cause we approve of its individualistic teachings. The
reply to Mr. Kipling’s arguments is very easy. The man
who works after-hours because he has a wife in esse or in
pvsse works twice as much in his employer’s interests as
in his own and her’s, and dead in the teeth of communal
interests under existing conditions. We want co-opera-
tion, not merely of men, hut of mankind, of men and
women—their interests are one, and their course is ob-
vious.— Ed. B.
M'Leod, doctor and preacher, now in
Darlinghurst prison undergoing a sentence of
three years for bigamy, has appealed to the Privy
Council. The appeal to the N.S.W. Supreme
Court was heard on 4th July last, when the Chief
Justice and Sir George Innes (Judge Windeyer
dissenting) upheld the conviction, but for different
reasons. The Bulletin predicts that Judge
Windeyer’s opinion will ultimately prevail against
that of the majority of the Full Court.
Governor Kintore has refused to grant the
Cockburn Government a dissolution. The position
is a peculiar one. The Ministry was defeated on
the Progressive Land Tax. Had they asked for a
dissolution then, it would almost certainly have
been granted. Subsequently Playford tabled a
motion of no confidence on general grounds—so it
is alleged—which was carried, but it is undoubt-
edly true that the Progressive Land Tax policy
decided the later voting as much as the former.
The point now is—Was Kintore justified in over-
riding his Cabinet ? It is stated that the Governor
consulted one of the judges. If he consulted
Chief Justice Way, then he consulted a man who
is financially interested in the Progressive Land
Tax Question to an enormous extent, as it would
touch him heavily were it carried through Parlia-
ment. Kintore has taken considerable responsi-
bility on his shoulders in going against his
Ministry.

The new German smokeless rifle, rivaling
the French Lebel, shoots a lead-pencil-shaped
missile which will go through six men or six
inches of brick wall. In 1848 the Prussians sprung
a surprise on Europe by accumulating army rifles,
which only became known through the Berlin
insurgents looting the arsenal.
