The Referee.
Not loll" since the N.S.W. Treasury was drawn
upon for a Registrar-General, in the person of “ Charley ”
i'lnhey, and now its resources have been further taxed by
the withdrawal of P. .1. Holdsworth, poet, cashier, and
man of fashion, who goes as Secretary to the recently-
constructed Forestry Department. No longer will the
guileless publican have the chance on the 30th of June of
ringing in the rupee and the Bank of England note upon
the wary Joseph. “ Joe's ”... weaknesses are epigrams and
ample frock-coats of gorgeous pattern. A tradition is
extant at the Treasury that one day “Joe” was passing
punch’s Hotel when he became inspired with a more or
less poetical idea, and fearful lest it should escape his
memory he stopped dead and took out his note-book to
iot it down. The book and the big check-pattern d;d the
trick, and P.J. was horrified by a query from one of the
unwashed as to “ what price he was laying the two Grand
Flaneurs?”
* * *
The Dawson-Maber fight for the 1 igl it weigh t-
ohampionship—or rather, the first event of the trials
to find the champion—proved a manly give-and-take
contest from the first to the twentieth round, when
Maher was beaten, more from exhaustion than the actual
punishment he received. Maher’s exhibition showed him
to be entirely devoid of ring cunning, and the writer
cannot hut think that if he had had a smart second, the
battle would have had a different termination. He never
from first to last made any attempt to fight at Dawson’s
body, and as the latter is clever at stopping, it was a
simple matter for him to stop Maher’s blows, which he
knew would go for the head. Dawson is a fair, manly
boxer, and a great favourite with the crowd, but as the
ironbark man, Burge, fought a draw with him, and a
lightweight such as “Dummy ” Mace stands in his way,
itreally seems premature to dub him champion. The
writer in all faith believes “Billy” Murphy could give
either Dawson or Maher their weight in and a drubbing,
and should “Jack” Hall, now in Melbourne, think it
worth while to get rid of his aldermanic proportions, he
ought to he able to thrash the pair before breakfast one
morning, just to give him an appetite.
# * #
At last the Australian scullers see the error of
their ways, and are going to the States to rake-in some of
the Yankee dollars which have been waiting for them so
many years. Kemp and O’Connor are to row in America
for £5OO aside and the championship next March. The
exact spot of venue is not decided, but we won’t charge
Mr. Deeble anything for the suggestion that tenders
should be called in America from the owners of the many
three-miles-with-a-turn courses, and we’ll guarantee the
highest tender will not fall far short of 10,000 dollars.
* # *
The most amusing sporting statement which
ever saw light came out last week when the papers
announced that Kemp and Stanbury, under the auspices
of the N.S.W. Rowing Association, are going to row two
miles on the Parramatta, “just to show the public which
is the better man.” Deeble, Kemp, and Stanbury are
bound on an American trip, but it seems they are getting
their hands in at the showman business before starting.
Ye gods ! the astute “ Johnny ” Deeble going to show his
hand for “nix” to the general puhlic! It makes us smile.
* * *
“Billy” K , the well-known racing man,
and his chum “ Jack,” many years ago when travelling by
rail to and from country meetings, used to keep a carriage
to themselves in a very simple manner. Should a stranger
attempt to enter “Jack” would feign a fit, and K
would cry out “ in the name of Heaven for some one to
come and help hold him down.” The game worked for a
long time, but at last the pitcher was taken once too
often to the well. At Campbelltown, one night, a big
lump of a fellow was seen approaching the door, when
“ Jack” went off into a superb fit. The newcomer hap-
pened to be a Sydney detective, who nearly choked Tohn
ere explanations could be made.
# # #
Kemp’s readiness now to row O’Connor in
America, and thus risk the loss of the championship,
shows how worthless were the quibbles raised by “the
stable” until they found that O’Connor was not good
enough for Stanbury.
* * *
Who, to look at Larry Foley now, would be-
lieve that he used to fight in his early days at a shade
over nine stone ? The pub. is the enemy of the athlete.
* * *
It seems we, in Australia, have been “ barking
up the wrong tree.” Jockey Boase says Kirkham’s miser-
able exhibition was caused through over- training, and that
both Narellan and Kirkham are now cripples.
* * *
The Australian cricketer at “home,” when
batting the day after a banquet, sees two balls (at least)
approaching. One is dead on the wicket. He smites at
the other, and then sees four bails flying about, two
wicket-keepers biting their gloves and looking the other
way, two prostrate and two erect stumps. Then he retires
to two pavilions, makes twenty-two excuses, and another
cable about bad luck and wet wickets is despatched. The
Australian always plays on a “wet” wicket after a
banquet.
* # *
It seems that Slavin refused to give an exhibi-
bition-spar in one of the English provincial towns
because he discovered, on his arrival, that the advance-
agent hadn’t billed him as much as he (Slavin) could
desire. Some of the papers snorted considerably at
Slavin’s original refusal to meet M’Auliffe on the ground
that the American giant “ wasn’t class enough.” Tom
Sayers and Co., it is remarked, were not too high-and-
mighty to take on a soft thing, when the soft thing was
ready to pay handsomely for being slaughtered. Slavin
might try to cultivate a little of the humble spirit of
scullerßubear, who came over here with the professed in-
tention of prospecting for somebody he could lick.
# * *
It is cabled that Lawrence Zucker, the well-
known Australian bookmaker, has died in England, of
brain-paralysis. He began business ns loblolly boy in
Barnard's tobacconist shop, Bourke-street, Melbourne,
when they used to do so much in list-betting. Gradually
he bloomed into a bookmaker, and became rich, but was
obviously not himself at last Cup, when he plunged
against Bravo, and his heart was broken. He will be
longest remembered for his V.R.O. disqualification ovei
the Newminster alleged “nobbling" business.
• # #
The three strongest supporters of long-distance
racing in England are Mr. W. G. Craven, Sir John Astley,
and General Owen Williams. These are also the three
unluckiest racing men on the English turf.
* # #
The hard-headed “ Pendragon,” writing of the
second Slanbury-O’Connor boat-race, says that after all
chivalry is not dead among professional scullers, and also
that it is pleasant to set Stanbury’s act in contrast to
“ the blowing and the gas which in Australia go, as a rule,
hand-in-hand with—not incompetency and dufferism, as
with us, but with real right-down first-chop talent, courage
and ability. According to such telegrams as have been
received here, it appears as though Stan bury, finding
O’Connor was dissatisfied with the result of the first race,
gave him another all in for the same stake as they had
rowed for before ; an act which has no parallel in this or
(so far as is known) in any other country.” Then, after
viewing the question with wonderful clearness, consider-
ing the meagreness of his information, he asks, “What
should we have thought of Kelly if, after a race with
Chambers, in which ‘hand awa’ Bob ’ was beaten, and of
course, angry and aggrieved, the Southoountryman had
said, ‘ Well, to prove to you I am the better man, and no
flies, I won’t reckon I’ve won this race ; we’ll regard it as
a dead-heat, and come out to-morrow or the day after and
row off the decider ?’ ” Or, he remarks, what of Elliott
and Boyd if they had thus settled their disputes, or of
Hanlan if he had thus given Trickett a second go for the
same stake once more'? lie might have said something
of Sadler and Kelly, who had a lengthy lawsuit over a
hundred pound stake, but forgetting that, states; “ Why,
we should have thought, in any one of these instances,
that the man who t hus risked what he had already won,
and ought to put in his pocket, was stark, staring mad,
and only fit to be placed under control of an asylum’s
authorities.” Scarcely satisfied with heaping all this jam
on the unaccustomed Australian, he then administers a
slight corrective, and dismisses the quixotic and high-
class sculler to go for Slavin, the boxer, “who has shown
himself a fairly good man—how good we cannot tell,
seeing the lack of force there has been in the trying tackle
hitherto obtained tor him ; but no one will say he has
shown any readiness to give points away or do any one
thing that might by the most optimistic be regarded as
generous, That Slavin is a brave man is absolutely
without doubt; if we had not received good proof of this,
we might have supposed after the Bruges affair that it
was Slavin, and not Smith, who stood forth as champion
empty braggart. For it must be confessed Slavin did not
bear his blushing honours at all modestly; quite other.
He seems to me to live for the purpose of practically up-
setting the popular belief that all men w r ho bounce are
only of the windbag sort, and that when such men parade
they are only covering up their cowardice. Slavin, as I
have said, has shown us he has pluck, but he has also
shown us he can blow like fort} 7 .”
# * #
It is said that some members of the Yorkshire
Eleven are paid so much extra by Lord Hawke while they
Seep sober.
# * #
Sculler O’Connor, who was banqueted the other
day by some rowing friends, was picniced down the har-
bour on Saturday last by Mr. and Mrs. Frank Coffee as a
sort of hon voyage. A certain great and illoquint politi-
cian was for some unaccountable reason absent on both
occasions ; however, no one w ill for a moment imagine
that it was because he is only the friend of the successful
man. Just before leaving by the Austral for London, the
Canadian was handed a gold watch by Mr. Toohey,
M.L.A., on behalf of his Australian friends, and was
cheered loudly by a large assemblage as the steamer cast
off from the wharf.
* # *
A Midland handicapper asserts that he has
heard of a father shaving in order to run in the same
boys’ race as his son.
* # *
The N.S.W. amateur skating championship over
a mile-and-a-half course was won by Detores in 4min.
S24secs., with Garland two yards away and Campbell 30
yards in the rear.
* * #
In the return match Britisher Bubear, conceding
lOsecs. to ex-amateur Kerr, was defeated by Hsecs. The
match was rowed on the Saltwater River, but smells strong
of the Yarra.
* # #
Neil Matterson had a second winning turn on
Monday last, .when he defeated his quondam pupil, C.
Stephenson, of New Zealand, for a stake of £2OO, by four
lengths, over the champion course, in 20 minutes 57
seconds, with wind and tide favourable. Neil is now
matched against Chris. Neilson, the conqueror of the Tas-
manian Stephenson, for £2OO aside, to row over the cham-
oionship course in five weeks.
* # #
The result of Chris. Neilsen’s race with H. A.
Stephenson, the Tasmanian champion, over a three-mile
course on the Mersey, with the tide running out at the
rate of five miles an hour, was never in doubt, except
wdien the little fellow lost three lengths through an eddy
carrying him in shore. Stephenson, who is 27 years of
age," sft. llin. in height, and weighed only lOst. 61b., says
he’ll never row again. He is certainly rather lightly
built for the game. Neilsen is three inches shorter, but
six pounds heavier, and rowed in a four pounds lighter
boat.
* * #
One reason for the yearly increasing strength of
the “fields” in big handicap races is, that owmers are
bribed to start cripples on the off-chance by the holders of
their mokes’ names in big sweeps. Thus Clements, the
great Sydney “ Tonic ” man, has entered a crook of some
kind or other for this year’s Cup, probably for purely
advertising purposes, and the man who gets “ Clements’
Tonic ” in the big Consultations will negotiate with
Clements for a start. If Clements wants to get a real ad.
for his money, let him hire a draught stallion, a saddle,
and a 28-stoiie jockey to appear at the starting-post.
# * *
Yalpunga is a small town near the S.W. corner
of Queensland, with the usual hqte!, store and black-
smith’s shop. Once a year, when races are held, it can be
likened to nothing but a “ small hell on earth.” The
handicappers at the last races were two cripples, one
being lame and the other, whose usual cognomen is
“ Wingy Dick,” having only one arm. One jockey was
very wroth about the weight put on his horse, and imme-
diately began to hunt for the unfortunate handicappers,
promising to leave them in such a state that their own
mothers wouldn’t know them. On the offenders being
pointed out to him he exclaimed, in accents of disgust,
“Oh! h— 1 They’re afraid to have men as handicappers.
These are only halves ! I’d have been satisfied if I could
have punched them.”
* * *
A ’Frisco man asseyts that John D. Spreckels,
the clever amateur boxer, who has so persistently backed
Jimmy Carroll, and who lost 5000dols. over the M’Auliffe
fight, can use up “Bob” Fitzsimmons with the gloves.
He says: “That slender amateur, who was an invalid
before ho took up athletics, just walked away with him.
He led and landed at will, ducked all of Fitz.’s leads, and
countered until he had the Australian silly, and showed a
knowledge of ring tactics and a number of the tricks of
boxing that simply astonished me. That Australian was
simply paralysed, and swore he had never seen a boxer
before.” Perhaps Pitz. was “ kidding,” but more probably
amateur is a good sort and takes newspaper
pinß in exchange for champagne suppers and cigars. We
want to see Spreckels fight Fitzsimmons after basely
alienating the affection of the latter’s best girl.
# * *
Mr. Goodyear, who is to pick the intercolonial
eight this year, wants to order a boat from Edwards, of
Melbourne. Leaving other builders out of the question,
a place which can boast a “Joe” Donnelly need not go
further afield, and the win should be for a N.S. Wales
boat as well as crew. However, as the crew has not yet
been picked for the race, which is to come off in November,
they might as well row in a dug-out as a racing-craft, for
they would have as much show of winning in one as the
other. The University eight should have been kept to-
gether to represent the colony.
* « *
Billy Murphy worked his way as a pantry boy
on board the Zealandia to America. He returned saloon
on the same ship worth two or three thousand pounds, it
is said. A ’Frisco authority, admitting his gameness and
wonderful hitting power, says that if “ Ike Weir’s hands
were sound he could trounce two Murphys in one ring.
He was compelled to fight Billy here with a bullet wound
through one of his fingers, and even thus handicapped he
made a monkey, a holy show, and a punching bag of the
‘Australian wonder,’ until his hands became so sore he
could no longer use them. Then, finding that Murphy
was comparatively strong and fresh, and that if he con-
tinued the struggle he was in for a first-class hiding, he
took advantage of the first punch in the stomach that he
received to go to the floor and double up like a jacknife.
He wasn’t out, but he could fight no more. This was
strictly true, for his hands were tlseless and he wasn’t in
the mood to let himself become a ohopping-blook, and so
he lets his seconds cry ‘peccavi’ for him. When the
affair was over, to save his reputation he quietly gave out
that the affair was a cross and that he had received
5000dol for losing.”
* * *
The totalisator will, in all probability, be soon
legalised in Victoria. Hitherto it has been blocked by the
influence which the big bookmakers exercised over both
branches of the legislature. The two leading bookmakers
arc now in England, but in any case the “ Leviathan ”,
does not now oppose the “ tote,” which is certain to kill
his form dable rival, the “ Toerag Tommy,” orsmall book-
maker. The infernal machine can never affect the big
fielder, for owners and commissioners will always find it
necessary to get on their money at a stipulated price,
which the totalisator could never guarantee. But it would
kill the vampires, who are now sucking the life-blood of
sport.
* * *
The second test-match between England and
Australia, played at Kennington Oval on a wet and sticky
wicket, was won by the former country by two wickets.
Australia scored 92 and 102 (Trott made 39 and 25), and
England put up 100 and 95. In the Leyton match with
Cambridge University, Past and Present, the Australians,
apparently right out of it on the first innings, were
deprived of a win by time only. The scores were—
Australia, 218 and 356 for 6 wickets (Murdoch 129 and 30,
Trott 38 and 186, Blackham 66); Cambridge, 389 and 78
for 8 wickets. The Portsmouth match with Oxford and
Cambridge, Past and Present, was also drawn, the
Universities scoring 233 and 112 for 2 wickets, and Austra-
lians 131 and 300 for 5 wickets. In the University matches
the Australians took advantage of the new rules to close
their innings on the last day.
* * *
A Caulfield trainer, on being arrested or. a
charge of borrowing money upon his employer’s horses,
told the detectives that Sir Wilfred would win the Caul-
field G.N. Steeple on the following day. Melbourne
Herald published the tip in its police-news, and, as it
came from a trainer, Sir Wilfred was a bit rushed that
night. He went out at 10 to 1 next day and won easily.
» * *
The omission of Attewell, Peel, and Briggs from
the last All England Eleven suggests not only that the
Britishers are well off for bowling talent just now, but
also that they should have no small pull in being able to
change their teams, at the last moment, to suit the
wicket. Martin and Sharpe, chosen as dirty-weather
bowlers, are, however, lengths behind either Peel
or Attewell with the bat, and seeing that such bats
as W. G. Grace and Walter Read were always pretty
helpless on a heavy wicket, the Australians had a
fair show to snatch the “brilliant” muddy victory
they narrowly missed. The kindest—or, perhaps, the un-
kindest—thing to say of the Australians batting this trip
is that they make as many runs against the best howling
on bad wickets, as they do'on good ones. With conditions
favourable for run getting they have made very few
decent scores against the professional attack.
THE LONDON POSTMEN.
There is no class of men on which the business
public are more dependent than on the London
postmen. They are entrusted with communications
of the highest importance, which rarely, if ever,
they fail to deliver promptly and on time. These
men, as a class, are sober, industrious, hard-worked
servants. From early morning until late at night,
in all weathers, they continue to tramp over and
over the same route. They become a part and
parcel of the business man’s existence Thi s
constant walking and exposure in this changeable
climate often entails rheumatic difficulties, pains
and stiffness in the limbs ; yet there can be no
cessation from the daily routine of their lives.
Whether suffering or not, they are obliged to be
at their post. It is said that a great proportion
of the London postmen always keep that famous
remedy, St. Jacobs Oil, by them, applying it to
the muscles after a severe day’s work as a pre-
ventive. Others who have received sprains
have been promptly cured by the application of
this remedy, and they not only use it upon them-
selves and families, but recommend it to others.
Mrs. J. Brooker, 47 Princess-road, Kennington
Cross, London, S.E., the wife of a postman,
writes; —“I have suffered most agonising rheu-
matic pains through my shoulders and the back of
my neck for some months past, many times being
unable to obtain any sleep at night, which wholly
unfitted me for my household duties in the day-
time. My husband having heard his fellow-post-
men speak very highly of St. Jacobs Oil for
sprains and soreness in the limbs, as well as for
neuralgia and rheumatism, procured a bottle from
the company’s office, at 45 Farringdon-road, the
contents of which he applied to the afflicted parts,
and to our great suprise and joy the pain departed
almost at once, affording me the first good night’s
sleep I had been able to obtain for many a night. I
consider St. Jacobs Oil an invaluable preparation for
pain, and shall always keep a bottle of it on hand.’>
A. F. Cann, of 31 Swinton-street, Gray’s Inn.
road, London, W.C. writes -“One morning on
my round, a< postman, I sprained my ankle and
was scarcely able to walk, when I was recom*
mended to use St. Jacob’s Oil. I procured a
bottle, and before I had used one-half the con.
tents I was perfectly cured.” Mr. H. Newman,
45 Picton street, Camberwell, London, S.E., post-
man, says: “Have suffered from rheumatism in
the knees for a period of twelve months, during
which time I consulted several physicians and
tried many remedies, without receiving any bene-
fit. Being advised to use St. Jacobs Oil; I hav e
much pleasure in saying that it eased the pain
directly. I felt it act like an electric battery on
me. The contents of one bottle completely cured
me, and I can now walk as well as ever. I hava
no hesitation in recommending St. Jacobs oil to all
suffering from rheumatism as being a ‘ Conqueror
of Pain.’” While the postman’s “lot at best is
not a happy one,” he is undoubtedly saved much
pain and discomfort, which he would otherwise be
obhged to endure, were it not for the remedy in
question.— Manchester Athletic News.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will he paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under :—
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drauxings m ust be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
U. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

the characteristics of the hand. Samples free at

The Lay of C. Divan.
I’m a bushman from the Bogan, whence I yester-
day came down
With a cheque to spend on liquor and the pleasures
of the town,
Yet I’m now without a “stiver,” a debauched and
broken man,
Which is owing to a fellow known to me as 0.
Divan.
After reaching Redfern Station I began to knock
around,
And I swallowed sev’ral boozes as a man of cash
is bound, ,
Then my ’baeca-pouch got emptied, and to fill it 1
was my plan,
And I stumbled at that moment up against “ Cigar
Divan.”
To the window I was tempted by a glimpse of
women fair,
Ev’ry known professional beauty being represented
there; *
But before the shop I entered I took care the sign
to scan
Where this name was painted up—oh, confound
that C. Divan !
In attendance at the counter was a nymph of
beauty rare,
Well-developed, painted, powdered, with a free
*and winning air,
Who said, “ Step into the parlour, oh ! you wicked,
naughty man !”
Weak, I followed her, expecting there to meet
with C. Divan.
Not a moment were we seated when she spoke to
me of fizz,
Then I found that, though unlicensed, yet she ran
a liquor biz. ;
And sh" drew the cork as deftly as the oldest
pub'dean,
While siie whispered That she loved me even more
than C. DiVah.
Nothing further I remember till I found myself
to-day.
Charged with ructions, at the Central, and I
heard the copper say—
“ Drunk, yer worship, quite incapable, uncon-
scious, an’l ran
Him into quod for safety.” Said I, “Oh, Cigar
Divan.”
Then the sergeant shouted “ Soilence ! are yiz
guilty, are yiz not ?
That s the question you must answer ’stead o’
talking foolish rot.”
And I nodded acquiescence, thinking ’twere the
better plan—
“Eoise av Coort,” the Bench retorted, while I
swore at C. Divan,
For my pockets now were empty, so ’twas entered
on the sheet
By the trap who found me lying in a stupor on
his beat; ' - , ~
P’r’aps ttie copper had been through me as
through many a better man,
But I b’lieve my stuff was collared by young Miss
Cigar Divan.
I was quickly liberated, for the sheet was light
that day, . , ...
And I smiled- as Black Maria bore my fellow-
drunks away; .
But the feeling turned to sadness, which to gall as
quickly ran , . ,
When I found how large the dealings of my mend ,
Cigar Divan.
One hundred such I visited and yet I could not
The h gay and charming siren who has been the
ruin of me ; . , T
To the Bogan back I’m going as soon as e er i
And ril ne’er again be euchred by that shark,
Cigar Divan.

\ great row is now going on concerning
the original authorship of certain opinions on
orange - cultivation, between William Olarson,
F L S., of Mildura, and David Crichton, of the
Victorian Department of Agriculture, in which a
charge and counter-charge of plagiarism have been
made. Reading the pamphlet published by the
former in self-justification, one is led to believe
that he has somewhat the best of the position;
though no one can say what may happen when the
Admirable Crichton’s thunder comes along.
“ Many innocent people, says the cable,
“ were killed during the revolutionary uprising in
the Argentine Republic.” Now that the fighting
is over in Buenos Ayres, this is the kind of talk
that may be heard when a bank-clerk meets a
friend : “ How did you get on in the Revolution .
“Pretty well. You see, I owed my tailor
dollars, and the villain threatened to complain to
my manager if I did not pay up. Well, I de-
nounced him as a partisan of the President, and
i the mob broke into his place and killed him, and,
i while they were pillaging, I burnt his books, so 1
am clear of that lot.” “ But, amtpo, it was too
bad to get the poor man killed for the sake of a
paltry debt. ” “ Pouf'. One must make something
i out of these revolutions. Also, I had a shot at old
i Garcia, the bootmaker, but, by bad luck, I missed
him.” “ You missed him !, Demomosl What a
| pity ! I owe him ten dollars myself.”
Ibsen and bis Critics.
Editor Bulletin.—Sir,—Will you permit me to
address a few words to Mr, Fred. J. Broomneld,
at whose disposal you recently placed two columns
of The Bulletin to allow him to air his views
upon “ Ibsen and his Critics ?” From the headline
of Mr. Broomfield’s letter, I had expected to meet
witli something controversial —something intel-
lectual -but, unfortunately, only found hterarv
vapourings. Mr. Broomfield’s insistence upon
“ voluntary union,” indicates plainly that the tie
whion he desires should subsist between the sexes
is that of free love. Fie ! Mr. Broomfield. Read
Artemus Ward, and profi by his experience of
the vagaries of those who belong to that cult. Ihe
ardour imported into Mr. Broomfield’s pleading
for voluntary union and the forcible vituperation
lavished upon a state of society with which he can
have no acquaintance point to one conclusion : Mr.
Broomfield must be a bachelor. Otherwise he
would not be so enamoured of the platform of un-
controlled relationship between the sexes. To
such a one promiscuity would doubtless be very
alluring. Fathers and brothers may have prosaic
objections to urge. Gan it be that Mr. Broomfield is
an enthusiast who rides hobbies to death, or who
accepts theories merely because they are the fig-
ments of the fallacy of some half-crazed visionary i
Or' is it that he is a recluse who from his retreat
derides at the products of worldly experience ?
Where he is most faulty is that he is not careful
about his definitions. A riotous state of mind,
induced by cloistered contemplation of the allure-
ments of free love, may account for his short-
comings in this respect. Will he explain, in ordi-
nary nineteenth-century prose, what he means by
“conventional marriage ?” Does he allude to the
formal ceremonial practised on the Continent,
where the parents are the arbiters of their chil-
dren’s fate? Or does he refer to the marriage-
customs ruling in Australia? If he intends to
deride the Continental marriage, I am with him.
But it seems more probable that he is tilting at
any form of contract between the sexes. No one
nowadays disputes that a woman ought to be at
liberty to marry the man of her choice, in order
that an “idealistically-perfect union” should
be celebrated. A man’s groom may, if the lady
be willing, ally himself to his master’s daughter.
Nothing unholy attaches to such a union, except
in the opinion of those who bend before the shrine
of Mrs. Grundy. But when choice, voluntary
choice, has been made, responsibilities ought not
to be burked. Where trouble would ensue, if Mr.
Broomfie d’s system were brought into force, wou d
be that any pretext would serve as an excuse for a
woman to leave her home and her children. It is
not an unusual thing for a woman to tire of the
company of her husoand because he lavishes too
much affection on her. One woman cannot be
happy unless she is the ruler, an ther unless she is
the subject. It is just the same with men, and it
is such aberrations in disposition which upset
social systems reared on the Broomfield type. All
this preaching about the degraded condition of
women is very amusing, for does not history
demonstrate that when women will, men become
puppets ? In the example I ha e given the
woman was not compelled to wed the groom
nor to beget children, yet if she imagined that
“ the identity of those feelings and passions ”
which, according to Mr. Broomfield, should con-
stitute the ideal marriage did not exist, she might,
upon the flimsiest excuse, leave her homfe. It is
no answer to say that the -husband-would be well
rid of bier. The point is, should she have this easy
means of escaping responsibilities which she volun-
tarily accepted? Mr. Broomfield has evidently
got this bee of free love in his bonnet. Let him
get a wife, learn to love her and teach her that she
is not a slave to passion nor an ornament, but a
true help-mate. When he has done this, let the wife
announce to him that, because he objects to (say)
her thoughtlessly plunging him into debt, there is
no mental affinity between them, and let her leave
him ! He may then realise the pan? o f separation,
and may recognise that theories afford but unsub-
stantial comfort to a household rent in twain by
some petty outburst of temper.—l am, &0.,
FRED. H. BATHURST.

My Dear Moorabinda, —
N.S.W. Horticultural Society held one of its periodical
floral outbursts at Sydney Town Hall vestibule on 13th
inst., when Lady Carington, with H.E. and brother
Rupert, declared it formally open at 4.30, and wore a
heliotrope poplin skirt with moire ribbons of the same
shade, velvet bodice edged with gold galon, bonnet in
gold straw and purple velvet, and carried a very choice
posy, the gift of the society. Mrs. Burdekin was also
present during the afternoon, as were most local
lovers of garden-stuff, though the tendency of
flower-show crowds trends greatly and naturally
to the thick-soled boots and phllocomed hair of
the professional gardener, who lurks beside his pot
exhibit, throwing glances of deepest envy at his hated
rival, guardian of the adjacent abnormal bachelor’s-button
or hyper-developed Scotch thistle. The show was mainly
camellias (which, easily-soiled, stiff and scentless, are not
my favourite blossom) of no very special size, although
the colouring was very pure. The floral-baskets were
fetching, though I didn’t like the first prize so well as an
apparently unappreciated hamper of buttercups and fern,
which was most artistic. An exhibit where all the pots
were fixed up in white wadding suggested a visit to one’s
dressmaker—or the earache, but a grouping of jonquils
and daffodils on green plush was absolutely charming.
Apropos of the camellia-show, I stood at the beautiful
hyacinth exhibits near a sylph-like maiden who had a
she-friend with her. The friend inhaled the scent of
a lovely purple bloom and the sylph followed suit, and
then, straightening herself up, remarked -fervently
“Whenever 1 smell hyacinths or tuberoses I want to hug
somebody.” What a priceless pointer tor her best boy to
get hold of!
I am told that one reason for the success of “ Paul
Jones ” choristers under the baton of Alfred Plumpton is
that he marks on the music where they are to take
breath, and this prevents much choral gurgling and
choking, as in ordinary cases the singer with the short
breath grows apoplectic in her efforts to hang on as long
as the lady with the wind of a blacksmith’s bellows.
Which reminds me that the “leading lady” sometimes
buttons Plumpton’s spotless kids before he hies him to his
orchestral throne. But which “ leading lady ? ”
Some people don’t know everything. Madame Marian
Burton, who, in private life, is Mrs. Waterlow (pronounced
by the precisians “Waterloo”), was shedding the light
of her smile on a small tea-party recently, when
one of the guests turned to her and asked in an amiable
and chatty way “ If she had been to ‘ Paul Jones’?” At
this juncture somebody kindly led the gentleman outside
and explained the case for his benighted benefit.
After an unusual lull, the Servant Question again rears
its hydra-head in the city of “ Arbour and Horgan.” “A
domestic ” writes to a daily bewailing an absence of hot
water in her bath, or something of the kind, and wildly
advocating a servants’ union, and “ A Missus” acridly re-
taliates in the next issue, so war is again declared. Most
of the trouble seems to arise over the infrequency of
Bridget’s accorded opportunities for courting the pensive
policeman by gaslight in the back-lane. “Missus” com-
plains of Ellen’s habit of slipping out to “post a letter” on
alternate nights in the week—and when you reflect what
heavy work girls undertake in shops and factories, where
“ notice ” isn’t saucily given because of some trivial dis-
agreement, then it becomes clear that domestic service*
under the same conditions with regard to evening leisure*
would be, perhaps, a peaceful and beautiful thing.
In Adelaide (hey have struck a successful plan—adopted
in Sydney by a few people, but not to such an extent as in
the flat city—that of “day-servants,” who are hired (by the
mon th, of course) to come at 7 a.m. and depart either before
or after the last meal, thus having their coveted evenings
always to themselves. This plan is only feasible in one.
help menages, but as the majority of Australian homes
are run by a single exile, the South Australian inspiration
is a noble idea. There is a Sydney suburban household
where the girl comes at 7 a.m. and leaves at 7.30 p.m.
The mistress has tried this plan four years, and has
changed servants twice only in that time, while her maid
is as jolly as a sand-hoy, and the china wreckage insigni-
ficant. The plan is an admirable one under either of two
sets of conditions ; it is a good one if you happen to
know for certain that your servant lives with her own
people when she is not at your house. It is also a good
one if you have no conscience.
Among the drawbacks to day-service are the facilities it
hffers the help with wide ideas on the subject of meum
and tuum for making off with nocturnal swags of articles
not easily missed. Another is that if an unexpected «nest
drops in after the departure of your Abigail, you have to
answer the door yourself, and prepare and hand round the
“ cakes and But an extra shilling or two will pro-
long your help’s sojourn on the premises if you expect an
invasion or an “ at home ” —and even if it won’t,'it’s better
to make your own coffee once a month than go without a
servant half the year.
The vice-regal enclosure at Flemington during Cup
week won’t lack furbelows this time, since the ladies
Dorothea Louisa and Estella Mary Hope, sisters of
Governor Hopetoun. are coming out per Britannia to
quiz the “colonists.'’ It is quite time that the young
spinster aristocrat dumped her hair-trunk on our shores
and went on a squatter-chase, just to give local gilded
youth an even chance of aggrandisement with their more
sociallv-fortunate sisters, who have coronets and things
laid at their feet every time we shuttle Governors, while
their malt compeers ore left to the untitled and home-
grown blandishments of the indigenous syren. But the
worst of it is that even when the unmarried damsel of
high degree does venture across the ditch under vice-
regal chaperonage she generally regards local eligibility
with a lion-on-your-old-stone-gates sort of glare that nips
his young affections at the bud and pulls up his honour-
able intentions by the roots, so that the last state of that
man is worse than his first. Nevertheless, Melbourne
masherdom must be on the qui vive at the idea, and I
feel that I am only doing it a common kindness in
hinting that moire dress-coat revers are quite doosid
form “at home,” and also that the best models still part
their hair down the middle when they aren’t too bald to
comb up.

The committee of the East Sydney Rowing Club deserve
congratulations of the deepest dye for the success they
made of their annual ball held at West’s, on 14th inst.
The rooms were decorated with oars tied with the
colours (blue and white) of the club, and the supper-table
was very specially decorated with flowers, fans, mirrors
and fairy-lamps. There were nearly 200 visitors present
who were carefully looked after by the übiquitous M.’sC.,
Messrs. Ackland and Gribben, and the dressing was
generally good. Mrs. Marshall wore flesh-pink silk and
green velvet with pink jetted lace; a white tulle gown
A MISSIONARY VISITS THE INTERIOR.
had panels of white brocade and was adorned with lilies
of the valley ; a fair girl in yellow tulle and silk was a
success, and a white cashmere and green velvet dress with
Medici collar edged with pearls, was a most fetching
costume. Among the guests was Sculler O’Connor—who
learnt all the dancing he knows in Australia, by his own
confession.
It is terrible to read that ** the newest Parisian dresses
are all copied from historical toilettes.” I sincerely hope
the fashion doesn’t strike Sydney. The mental picture o*
a-fifteen-stone matron who will get herself up to repre-
sent Cleopatra is enough to frighten a person interested
in contemporaneous clothing. And then the anxious
question arises, how far back will the fair seeker after
novelties go in search of a killing idea ? When the hot
summer comes, the airy attire of our alleged lady pro-
genitor would commend itself to the woman who has a fig-
tree in her back-yard, while the poetical, filmy draperies
of mythology offer a solution of the problem how to be
cool and stylish to the girl who borrows Paris fashions.

Lord and Lady Carington will visit Waggaon 3rd prox.,
returning to Sydney two days later. They are going
merely at the pressing invitation of the townspeople, and
neither will be asked to open anything—not even a cham-
pagne-bottle. As Wagga is the Claimant’s former home
and is much better known by name in England than Syd-
ney, Baron Carington would hardly dare return to “the old
grey house near the grave of John Hampton ” without
being able to say he had visited the City of Crows.
The Carington Centennial Hospital for Convalescents
at Camden will, on 20th inst., be opened by Lady Caring-
ton for the reception of patients. Lord Carington will be
there, and is to open the Masonic Convalescent Hospital
shortly afterwards.
The annual Masonic ball of the lodge Sydney Tarbolton
was held in the Town Hall Vestibule on 12th inst., when,
despite the sloshy streets and dismal drizzle without,
there was a very good attendance indeed. A cream silk
with tulle draperies and pearl tablier was pretty, and a
pale.pink cashmere over apple-green was fresh and well-
fitting. The excellent music was under the b&ton of
“Brother” De Groen, and the catering was good.
Thirteenth inst. in the Horgan city was decidedly the
dilettante day out, since a picture-show, a flower-show,
and an Orpheus concert all took place one on top of
another. The Anglo-Australian loan collection of
pictures at the York-street Rink—which was wonderfully
transformed for the occasion—was alleged to be opened
by Lord and Lady Carington at three p.m., but although
the vice-regal twain arrived as per advt., attended by
brother Rupert in all his glory, no bored vice-regal bark
wafted down the listening breeze, and it is Sappho’s
rooted conviction that the thing was set going by a yawn
on the one hand and a smile on the other. But that made
no difference, anyhow. Some of the works of art don’t
merit the catchy rapture people indulge in so fearlessly in
front of each frame, but on the whole the show soothes a
barbarian “kawliny” whose appreciation of art is only
one stage ahead of coloured almanacks. The light is
very good and groups of palms in pots, terra-cotta busts
plaques and tables set with bric-ii-brac give the gallery a
gay and festive appearance—the only discrepancy being
in the numbers of chairs provided, there being rather too
few for an art show where one generally feels like the
hypochondriac in the “ Pickpocket ” all the time. There
was a very fair attendance. Besides the Government
House gang there were Mrs. Burdekin, in shades of helio-
trope ; Mrs. J. R. Ashton, in Indian red cloth and black
velvet, with black felt feathered hat; Mrs. P. B. Lark, in
a very special Paris frock in ecru woollen striped with
white, relieved with brown velvet, nobby hat in ecru
ostrich-feather sand brown velvet; and a girl in Indian-
red cloth braided in black, black Medici collar and hat in
red, black-jet and black birds—took all one’s attention off
the walls. And Alfred Plumpton was there wearing a big
diamond ring and pin, and expressed his intention o
criticising the pictures another day ; and the Rev. Charles
Clark went about with his hands behind him d la school
child. A special feature of the gallery will be gratis after-
noon tea “on alternate afternoons.” The deep sagacity—
not to say craftiness—of this announcement has only to
be understood to be appreciated.
I wonder what base motive actuates a draper when he
fills his windows with spring fixings in the middle of
winter? To see a shopful of blooming flowers and gauzy
hats when you are conscious of a westerly-wind nose and
wet feet isn’t a pleasing sight, though the present floral
outburst on the part of the Sydney shops isn’t as aggra-
vating as it would be if flower hats hadn’t been sported
all the winter. I suppose the primary object is to make
thoughtless people believe that beautiful Spring 1 is here,
and rush and buy a hat constructed of two-and-a-halt
wires covered with gauze and held together by a tulle
butterfly and a bugle, only to see it go sailing over the
street on top of a howling blast “from the mountains”
accompanied with sleet, the first time they put it on. As
one swallow doesn’t make a toper, neither do a hundred
airy hats in a window herald the presence of the poetic
season. They only signify that the shopkeeper has a
heap of 11 old lots” to dispose of with the aid of inspired
labels, while the real “novelties ” and “ as worn now ” are
being kept dark on the weather bow.
At most “ hall” concerts held in Sydney, the Y.R. party
have a dreadful time of it. They are always placed in the
very first row, about a yard off the trouble, and no matter
if the bill includes cymbal and big-drum solos, there they
must sit in stolid martyrdom in the very heart of the row',
and in the very worst position for hearing possible ! I sup-
pose the reason is that it wouldn’t be “ de rigger” to have
people sitting ahead of the Governor on the same level,
and so “one has to suffah for one’s ordah, yerknow.”
A preliminary meeting in connection with the proposed
“ Home for Governesses” was held at Sydney Town Hall
on 15th inst., under the presidency of Mayoress Burdekin-
Nothing definite was arrived at except that such an estab-
lishment was needed, and as the principal movers in the
scheme leave town shortly for an indefinite period, the
“ home” will, lam afraid, long remain in the sky. Offers
of help and cash-insults will be received by Mrs. Burdekin
at the Town Hall.
Everybody should have worn a black gown at the
final-brethren-and-farewell Orpheus concert at Sydney
V.M.C.A. on 131 b inst., for I’m sure all felt like crape and
cashmere and weepers. The opening Rheinberger quartette
was a very special thing indeed. Rivers Allpress played
an "Andante'’ by Poussard, accompanied by the com-
poser, and both had “ to bow their acknowledgments,”
and Kowalski rioted through a Chopin item in a way tha
sent an electric thrill from the soles of your boots to your
chignon. A lady from Camden, in yellow and white silk,
adorned in a decorative way with yellow roses and autumn
* leaves, was unwise in attempting the “ Prayer ” from
Tannhauser, while remembrances of Saville’s soft top
notes were in the memory of most present—and this lady’s
habit of vocalising through her clenched teeth suggested
the idea that she felt sea-sick. Miss Hoffman, in cream
silk, sang weirdly in a duet with “ Daddy,” but as
C. Huenerbein had just trickled through his “Berceuse,”
most of the audience were too sleepy to resent anything
but an alarm-clock. There was a crowded, well-dressed
house led by Lord and Lady Carington, Miss Walenn, and
Captain Trench, the vice-regal gown being a train of crim-
son satin brocaded in grey-green design, over a front of
pale, grey-green satin, mantelette in red velvet and cloth
with ridged sleeves, diamond spray in the hair and mana-
gerial bouquet of snowdrops and camellias and ferns.
Miss Walenn wore black silk and net gown with crimson
plush mantelette. And Baron Carington wore his hair
parted down the centre and whiled away the time whis-
pering waggish comments to Lady C., which seemed to
amuse her greatly. Speaking of concerts, at a very high-
class warble held in Sydney recently, a breathless pause
fell “in front” when it was comprehended that the
narrow shoulder-strap of a lady vocalist was slowly slip-
ping from grace. It was an exciting time, but when the
strap got fairly on the down grade the lady grasped the
situation with her side eye and hitched it backed to duty.
Nevertheless the prospect before she did so made many a
blushing young man hide his face in his programme for
the rest of the evening.
Miss Achurch was “ at home” by invitation to a crowd
of people at her residence, “ Vittoria,” Woollahra, on 15th
inst. Among those present were Sir George and Lady
Innes, Judge and Mrs. Windeyer, the Misses Allen, Mrs.
Garran—seventy guests in all. Ibsenish conversation,
refreshments, and the wonderful baby were the com-
ponent parts of the spree, which was so successful that
Mrs. Charrington intends holding another and a larger
one shortly, when a “ novelty” is promised, at the name
of which I am not permitted to hint.

It is stated that blondes are fading away,
dying out, both in England and America. But
what is a blonde? Was there ever such a thing?
Well, there was, but aureoline, at half-a-guinea a
phial, answers for a good deal of it. The wife of
a bookmaker whom the world knows well, was
first brunette, then blonde, then brunette again,
and now she’ is blonde once more.

Land of the Crocodile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Well-kuo \Vn in Brisbane was that patriotic
old “ Hielantnan,” “Frazer the Piper,” whose
proud Celtic ancestors pulled round the ark in a
boat of their own,” and danced to the tune of
“ Ghillie Galium " on the first patch of dry land
that left room enough for a Hielanman s foot.
Frazer was once returning from a Warwick show,
and he broke the monotony of the rail way-journey
to Toowoomba by playing a series of warlike airs
on the “ baagpeeps.” On arrival at Toowoomba he
stepped out on the platform and struck-up Hie
March of the Cameron Men.” An intelligent
bobby, suffering from superfluous intellect, strode
indignantly up to the old Highlander and told
him to “Shtop that damm row there!” Old
Frazer simply glared at him, in speechless,
measureless contempt. The “ trap ” said ‘ I com-
mand yez to hould yer whistle, in the name of the
people av Toowoomba !” Then the aged musician
mournfully folded up his instrumental orchestra,
and turning to a brither Scot, instructed him as
follows ;—“ Here, Tonal, ye’ll tak the pipes an’
lock her by! She’ll ga doon ta Brisbane in ta
mornin’ an’ she’ll buy Toowoomba, an’ she’ll pay
for her, an’ surely ta God she’ll get leave ta play
ta pipes in her ain toon !” All of which is Gospel
truth.

How many people know that Bathurst
burr was introduced to Australia by two brothers
named Abercrombie, who brought over wild Pam-
pas horses from .Valparaiso in 1862 ?
* * *
Rolf Bold re wood has compiled an incompre-
hensible but most readable hash out of that story
of the Bowen Downs cattle, in his “ Robbery
Under Arras.” It reminds me of the small boy’s
essay oh Chaos, “a great big heap of nothing,
and nowhere to put it.” I. will now give you the
first-published account of that remarkable trans-
action, obtained from authorities who cannot be
questioned,
* * *
Redmond and his friends took up some country
fo side Bowen Downs. The stolen mob of Bowen
Downs cattle had been driven on to the edge of
the run by some Bowen Downs acc mplice, and
some 1200 head were taken clean away by Red-
mond to South Australia. Among the lot was a
slab-sided white bull, covered with brands, pur-
chased from Archer, of Graeemere station, near
Rockhampton. The manager of Bowen Downs
at that time was Mr. Ranken, brother-in-law
of B. D. Morehead. So cunningly were the
cattle removed that some time elapsed before
Ranken became conscious of anything wrong.
The white bull from Graeemere was the only
animal the sale of which could be traced to
anybody, and it was apparently traced to
Redmond, who was arrested and locked-up at
Blackall, and remanded twenty-eight times, these
remands extending over a period of six months,
merely on the word of a policeman who believed
Redmond was guilty. At the end of the six
months he was discharged, and re-arrested at
Warialda a few weeks afterwards, brought before
the P.M. there three times, and on each occasion
admitted to bail, himself in £4OO and two sureties
of £2OO each. Bail was always obtained. From
Warialda he was remanded, on warrant, to Roma,
where he was released on bail to appear at Roma
in six weeks. Bail was granted by George Lukin,
P.M., now Police Magistrate at Maryborough.
In the meantime the white bull was brought
round by sea from Adelaide and placed in a pad-
dock at Mount Abundance Station, five miles from
Roma. At the end of the six weeks Redmond
appeared in answer to bail, and Police-Inspector
Nutting applied for a remand for eight days. Red-
mond asked for bail, which was granted by Lukinin
opposition to the police, who were unable to give
any reason why it should be refused. At the end
of eight days a packed bench refused bail to Red-
mond, who thereupon applied to the Supreme
Court, and a mandamus was issued by Judge
Lutwyche compelling the Roma Bench to grant
the bail. At the final trial in Roma, at the Dis-
trict Court, the chief witness against Redmond
was the Adelaide man to whom he was alleged
to have sold the white bull. The trial was
before Judge Blakeney, with Pring as Crown
Prosecutor—he had been sent up for that
purpose tor'replace Healy, as more likely, to
■#cure a conviction. One of the witnesses was a
mJn brought out of aN.S.W. lunatic asylum, sup-
posed to be one of Redmond’s party. He broke
down under cross-examination, after swearing the
bull was one of a mob taken by Redmond from
Bowen Downs. The evidence showed that if the
South Australian witness had received £2OO he
would have cleared out before the trial, and gone
home again. Redmond was ably defended by
barrister Paul, now the genial Judge Paul of the
Queensland District Court. The jury returned a
verdict of “ Not Guilty !” So that 1200 head of
cattle were taken away and sold, four men tried
for the offence—and nobody convicted !
• * #
One result of this trial Was that the
Government abolished the criminal jurisdiction of
the District Court at Roma, for Judge Blakeney
averred that no jury there would convict horse or
cattle-stealers. In order to save the civil sittings
of the Court, the lawyers arranged a dummy case,
which was withdrawn when the Court opened.
When Judge Blakeney was leaving, the whole
adult male population of Roma turned out in black
clothes with white handkerchiefs, surrounded the
Judge’s coach, and pretended to weep bitterly. On
that very day the criminal jurisdiction of the
Court had been restored by wire, but was with-
drawn again when the “weeping episode” was
reported to the Government, One of the funniest
facts in this interesting drama was the trial of
one of the four accused, three years afterwards,
for horse-stealing, before his former counsel, who,
in his judicial capacity, gave his old client 18
months’ hard labour ! This was the unkindest
cut of all !
# # *
One who was present at the trial tells me
that “ Redmond was one of the best bnshmen in
Australia. He looked like a prosperous Methodist
parson, and came into court with a long black
frock-coat and a cotton umbrella, clean-shaved,
except a little side-whisker, with a very demure
and sanctified expression. He looked about 40
years of age, atid sft. Gin. in height, and weighed
about 13 stone. ” These are solid facts, somewhat
different to the picturesque narrative in “ Robbery
Under Arms !” However, fiction has a big
license.

The Australian trade in kanakas is now
near its termination. The first South Sea Islanders
were brought to New South Wales by the V elo-
city, in 1847, for Mr. Ben. Boyd. There were 66
men and 4 women. The year 1890 is the period
beyond which no 1 about vessel Shall bring any
mote kanakas to Queensland, and there is not
much prospect of that privilege being extended.
Three years more will terminate the engagements
of the last “boys” imported, and then .must the
sugar-planters face the problem of “white labour
or collapse ” !
* * *
I was present Recently at a luncheon given
in Brisbane to J. L. Toole. His powers of con-
versation and oratory would not have made his
name known to the people in the next yard. He
only told one anecdote, and that was common
property among the apprentices at the forge of
Tubal Ca n. He reminded me of Alexander in
the studio of Apelles.
MAROOGALINE.
Two gentlemen in V ictoria ate arguing a
question about some sheep, and are acquiring ruin
and information simultaneously. One of them, it
is stated, trespassed with the sheep on the other s
land, and the other came out and chased them olf.
He is said to have put a good deal of unnecessary
force of expression into his conduct: anyhow, the
man with the sheep sued him for damage, and
needless velocity, and wear and tear, and so on.
He recovered one shilling, Olid the judge ordered
him to pay all costs, amounting to nearly £1000,
so that his victory promised to leave him £009 to
the bad, and then he spent about £500 more on an
appeal to the Full Court. The Full Court re-
versed the order as to costs, on the_ ground
that the judge was short of jurisdiction, and
decided that the plaintiff should pay his own costs,
some £600—and that the defendant should settle
the rest of the invoice. Next the defendant
made an expensive application to Judge in
Chambers demanding permission to appeal to the
Privy Council, and spent a good deal of money in
finding-out that the Judge in Chambers didn t
know whether he could appeal in such a case or
not. Finally he was directed to waste anothei
large sum in asking the Full Court whether it was
competent to carry the case to England or not, and
a listening world still awaits the result, ihe bill
is getting well on towards £3000 by this time, and
both parties begin to feel that at would have been
cheaper to settle the matter pugilistically, and
then make a common assault-case out of it.
Twenty years after the -Battle of Worth?
says the German Emperor, the last missing frag-
ment of German soil lias been peacefully added to
German territory. Thus wrote the German poet,
Carl Tanner, in 1807 :
The Lost Child.
On the main, on the glorious German main,
The wind and the wave both loud complain,
And ev’rv wave it carries along
Of our long-lost child the plaintive song,
Red is the strand,
White is the sand,
Such is our German Helgoland.
O Germany i ot mothers best,
Thou gather’st thy children to thy breopt,
O ne’er forget thy smallest child,
Exposed to the wave and tempest wild.
Red is the strand, &c., &c.
As swells the sea to mountains high,
And fills each stream beneath the sky,
So swell those words and fill my heart
With longings wild and bitter smart.
Red is the strand, &0., &o.
And art thou poor, and art thou small,
Thy mother’s thoughts are still with thee all
Till the happy day (and ’twill surely come)
When the lost child smiles in its father s home.
Red is the Strahd, &c., &c.
The House of Lords was struck by light
ning recently, but it is painful to reflect that
nobody was in it at the time, so that the effort
was in a great measure thrown away. It-the
Upper Chamber could be knocked into chaos
it would be the beginning of a new and
brighter era for the Weary l/mpire; but, uu-
happily, there are seldom more than seven peers
in it at one time, and even though the whole seven
could be burst and crumpled up by some devour-
ing element, so that nothing would remain except
a pint of hair upon the floor, what are they among
so many? The House of Lords doesn’t need
more than half-a-dozen or so to form a quorum,
and when these sifc exhausted snags aie
gathered together they can block the pro-
cess of the nation all by themselves and
theft get away befote the lightning can hit
them, and the lightning is a bad shot anyhow, and
generally strikes some hard-working person with
a large family. The fact that not a single British
monarch was ever thunderblasted on the head,
and that the one solitary king m all the British
Isles who died of leprosy was pretty nearly the
only individual of his class who was worth preserv-
ing, has done more to undermine our religious
convictions than anything else. And just because
a peer is a mere imbecile obstruction and never
se; ved any useful purpose, it follows that a peei
could be dropped over a precipice 850 ft. deep, and
hit on the head with a shot-tower afterwards,
without suffering any injury; while a patriot
and a philosopher would break Ins neck
if he rolled-off a verandah 2ft. tun. high-
And if the peer in question was on hw
way to vote that the royal salary should be
raised and that the Coercion Act should be ex-
tended all over the empire, lie would arrive at the
House before the division-bell rang, even though
eleven tbavs loaded with railway iron went over
him on the road ; whereas, if he wanted to intro-
duce a measure for the nationalisation of -and, he
would accidentally sit down on a tack, and moiti-
fleation would set in next day. Ihe moral ot this
is that lightning doesn’t strike the House ot Lords
often enough to do any good, and if the nation
relies oh that Uncertain element, instead of em-
ploying some weapon that isn’t quite soAflow m
the operation, it will certainly get left.

At the Sign of the Shorn Sheep.
(FOR T HE BULLETIN.)
Before railwn had brought new-chum
labour to the door of the pastoral lessees, before
the iron-horse bad given M‘Muck and Co. power
to pen up a mob of “ scabs ’’and transport them to
the fair skin of Riverina, before the great Uir
known Reserve was thrown open to selection, in
fact, before it was generally known that a legiti"
mate prey was easily caught at “ the shearing ”
before all this, sharpers were few and far between*
and few spielers “ travelled on the game ” among
the squattages. But when digger “Hats” ran
short, when new-ehums (with coin) were superin-
tended by the police, military and club gentlemen
of Melbourne and Sydney, then the “fly” man
bethought him that there must be money, loot*
hoot, greed, stuff, sugar, dibs, possibles, and even
spondulicks where the busbies’ cheques came from.
Some accordingly learned to shear, some to roll
wool, and many to pretend to do the same. And
year by year the huts at shearing*time became less
safe to keep a pound in, though clothes, watches,
guns, and saddles were still seen lying about.
Then these excrescences returned to the slums of
Colling wood, Carlton, Woolloomooloo, and Light-
square and indiscreetly told their quondam mates
of their success. The railway and tree-passes did
the rest; the flood of employed unemployed set in,
and it became unsafe to own a silk handkerchief,
a meerschaum pipe, or a spare suit of clothes in
the general sleeping huts. At some stations
(always large ones) the mob of “lads,” sometimes
called “ talent,” were far and away the majority,
at others the older bushmen held the sway, and on
small stations the town sneak had to keep up a
fair disguise.
* # *
I am about to describe to you two men of
a class that is hybrid between the bushman who
never sees town and the professional “ gun.’ You
must learn the slang, where I do not explain it,
by reference to someone who has “ been there,” or
by taking a trip to the Riverina. There are many
men from Melbourne, Sydney and Adelaide who
do a fair amount of work in the off-season in town,
and who would not miss the shearing and harvest
on any account. Some of these men are a cut
above ordinary “wiring,” but will cheat at cards,
and ring in a “nob” or “grey” at the heading-
school. The “ talent ” say they are afraid to prig,
but in the end I think the half-and-halfs, who
keep pretty well within the law, have the best of
it. They are not so often lime-burners (synonym
for “stone-brokers”), for signification whereof
ride slang-dictionary. Or, if you haven’t a slang-
dictionary, ask Parkes. He has been there.
# * *
Two-more dissimilar mates than Tommy
the Ringer and Tyke could not be found. The
ringer at a shed is the man who tops the list of
fast shearers. Tommy never shore a sheep in his
life. But once he had his name down for a pen,
and got a pair of shears, a stone, and a bottle of
oil. When the bell sounded he caught a sheep
and—let it go. The manager was near and knew
at once that (as a shearer) Tommy was a fraud.
He was quickly put on the rouseabout staff and
supplied with a broom. If he had been an ordi-
nary sweeper he’d have been dubbed “broomy n
or “sweeny,” but the unshorn sheep got him a
higher title, ironically “The Ringer.”
* * *
Who does not know Tommy the Ringer ?
1 mean that anyone who comes out of
Melbourne to Igok for a shed, can tell you
ail about Tommy and Tyke and their sayings
and.doings. Tommy was, and is, one of the best-
looking men in Melbourne. Even when “ Mops-
and-brooms ’ he was always clean and well-dressed.
Where he got his education and address I do not
know ; some say at the University, others at the
harm, wherever that may be. Certain it is that
he can translate Virgil and solve a quadratic, and
he reads politics instead of bogus championships.
A very different mart is Tyke. He first met
Tommy at one of Big Clarke’s stations, which they
sundowned after carrying the swag out of Mel-
bourne. Tommy was green, so was Tyke ; but
the latter had cheek, and faced every available
chance of cadging provender, and he shared it
with Tommy. Tyke knew what he was doing.
He thought that a well-dressed mate would do to
work the houses, kitchens', and the upper-ten when
fairly on tramp. Tyke was terribly dirty. I
don’t think the dirt was so real as apparent. He
never bought clothes. His town-suit was always
pawned or stowed away, and disinterred on liis
return. He was the prince of cadgers. His
coats were always too large, and were ragged at
the sleeves. He had no hats. You could not
honestly call his “cady” anything but a “cady ”
or “canbeen.” His trousers were picked up in
shearers’ huts, and his boots outside the same.
As for his shirt, not shirt, a footballer’s guernsey
which he begged off some triumphant player, that
was his nether garment; blue and white in the
beginning of shearing, brown and black later on >
until he was worried to wash it. A dip in the
wool-scour brought it back to its pristine colours.
* # #
These two independent variables became
mates, as I have said, and got into the same shed.
I have ttold you of Tommy’s entrance. Tyke
became “slushy,” or “cook’s mate.” They did
not spend much money. Tommy won a pound or
two at cards, and was accused of cheating. Tyke
won a few shillings and was growled at for “sock-
ing the stuff.” Then they repaired to the neigh-
bouring township. There the respectable-looking
Tom got drunk and lost all his money ; Tyke said
he had done the same. Then they got a second
shed and the same thing happened. Then they
went harvesting in Victoria and drank most of,
their money. Bendigo was what Tyke was pleased
to call his home; they stayed there a day and a
night, but Tyke refused to leave until about 12,
noon, next day. Tommy wanted to prospect for
more work, and went to look up the farmers at
some hotels. Soon he saw Tyke coming out of the
Post-office and went to ask him what Ire had seen
there. The answer was vague, but Tyke was now
ready to start, and they travelled on harvesting
and doing odd jobs until they reached Melbourne.
Here Tyke toolc to fish-hawking, and Ids mate got
a job as clerk in a grocery. That is all I have to
tell you about their first year. Their second was
like it, and they appeared to be still vast fr ends.
When Tommy was “broke,” the Tyke would always
lend hiin threepence or sixpence to get a pint, but
would invariably seek and obtain payment of the
same. Tyke would always drink if the drink was
free, but never paid or shouted for liquor. It was
during this year that the name was given him.
The Scotch overseer of a station said, “ Here’s this
tousy lookin’ Tyke again. We’ll gie him his auld
job,”
* « *
Strange to say, the Ringer’s money went
as before, and hi i chum declared that he also had
been bitten. Aid again they passed through
Bendigo; but Imre the better-looking and really
sharper of the pair made two discoveries. The
first wa,s that his mate was banking money in the
P.O. Savings Bank, and the second was that Tyke
could get drunk and still appear to be sober, and
even forget what he had done when drunk. Then
they went their ways again as before, and we see
them at the third exodus. Tommy was parti-
cularly civil to Tyke this year. He trusted him
with all his money, got it back from him when in
a township, and had it stolen as usual by Tyke,
who “socked it.” But Tommy had been studying
in the Public Library during his stay in the big
smoke, and had learnt a thing or two. On visiting
Bendigo ho refused to leave on the morning after
their entry. He carefully shephered Tyke until
ho saw him bank his money, and then he proposed
to have dinner at a public-house, and he (Tommy)
would pay. He also shouted a bottle of beer, and
then told Tyke that he knew he had a pound or
two in the bank, and could show him how to make
an honest livelihood by hawking, in which the
profits were more than cent, percent. Taking him
to the various merchants, and explaining to them
what he wanted, the Tyke entered into the scheme
and agreed to draw thirty pounds for a start.
i Tyke slept the sleep of the biter-bit,
and Tommy had a good look at his bank-book.
Only fancy, the miserable little wretch had
more than three hundred pounds in the
savings-bank! _ But it is necessary to give
a day’s warning before money can be drawn
at the P. 0., so the question was how to get Tyke
to give the warning and draw the money, for Tom
meant him to draw the lot. At last the latter hit
on a plan. He allowed his victim to sober up and
explained to him that it was silly to leave the
money there where he could not easily get it. He
did not know how much Tyke had, but if he put
it m an ordinary bank he would get a much higher
interest on his average monthly balance and could
make a better appearance with wholesale mer-
chants by giving cheques. Why not withdraw his
com, put it in a good bank, and do a week or two’s
work about the district so as to learn the best
markets? This was done and still the Tyke
thought that Tommy had no notion of the amount
he had in the bank.
* * *
On the evening of their return to Bendigo,
Tyke got Tommy to draw out a cheque, having
obtained a blank at the bank. But Tommy had
also found one in the office of the Shamrock, and
it was this latter that he filled up for £25. He
then filled up the one he had got at the bank for
£3OO and put it in a book on the table. After
Tyke had signed the smaller cheque Tommy asked
him to state definitely how they were to get on
together—were they to be mates, or was Tommy
to be only a servant ? Tyke said he’d give £1 a
week for a month and see how they got on. On
this Tom shouted bottled beer and went to fetch it.
About an hour afterwards he asked why Tyke had
not signed the cheque. Tyke said he had ;
Tom said he had only tried his pen on a
BCr ?’;P ~°f note-paper, and showed him some
scnbblmgs and produced the unsigned cheque.
Tyke the beer had got in his
head, and he signed the other one. Next dav
Tom asked if be remembered signing* the cheque.
Of course he did ; so they had two bottles of beer
at Tom’s expense, and about one o’clock went to
the bank. Then the moneyed man confessed he
was “ screwed,” and when Tommy presented the
cheque he leaned his head on the counter. The
cheque was passed and cashed, and Tom gave
Tyke £25 outside the bank-door, and repaired to
a hotel. From this time until a week or so after-
wards we have no accurate record of the doings of
this pair. In fact, I have only guessed at the
above facts. I can swear to the delivery of a
blank cheque to the Ringer; also to his having
gone with his mate and received three hundred
pounds in gold. It is also known that for a week
the two spent money in various places, and, arriv-
ing in Melbourne afterwards, they quarrelled
because the Ringer said that Tyke had lost and
squandered a fortune in a week and must have
planted a couple of hundred or so. “ Two.hun-
dred devils!" said Tyke “how much did 1 draw ?”
“Three solid centuries,” and refused to lend
me a tenner.” Tins sobered up the Tyke
in Earnest, for he had only four pounds in
his pocket. A telegram to Bendigo soon
proved that the Ringer spoke (apparently)
the truth, and from that day the Tyke has been"a
helpless, harmless, drivelling, idiotic sneak. JR,
spends his time in poking about the backyard of
the “ Shorn Sheep,” of which hostelry a word. It
was once the “Spotted Hog,” and the landlady
was a very nice, lady-like, little widow. She fell
desperately in love with one Thomas Tonsor, a
bushman who patronised the bar. The latter
proposed and soon became landlord. A few dabs
of white paint made the maculate poodle into
what is supposed to be a Shorn Sheep : hence the
sign. _ Just before marriage Tonsor said he won
£250 in a sweep. At least he produced that sum
in gold. Out of compassion for a poor old chum
who had gone balmy , he gave shelter and broken
victuals to a curious-looking animal in the back-
yard—a biped who is boots, messenger, wood-
chopper and watch-dog in one ; in fact, a faithful
hound. He answers to the name of Tyke. I can
recommend bushmen to the “Shorn Sheep Hotel.”
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
The London Institute of Actuaries pro-
poses to dispense with the services of medical
men in examining for insurance in cases where
the applicant prefers to make a statement before a
responsible officer. This break away from the old
rule is based upon the idea that thousands of
persons who, though nervous, are in good health,
will not insure because they have to “pass the
doctor, ”
Through the magnificent influx of sub-
scriptions, following the death of Dean Fitz-
patrick, tenders are called for the entire completion
of St. Patrick’s R.C. Cathedral, Melbourne,
except the towers, of which there will be three-’
two in front, each 250 feet high, and one centra],
350 feet high, at the junction cf the cross. The
spires will follow, on all three,, as a work for the
next century.
